One has to be twice as careful with a boy. Oh, you progressive\r\ndullards! You don\xe2\x80\x99t understand. You harm yourselves running another man\r\ndown.... But if you want to know, we really have something in common.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to know what.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it\xe2\x80\x99s all about a house-painter.... We are getting him out of\r\na mess! Though indeed there\xe2\x80\x99s nothing to fear now. The matter is\r\nabsolutely self-evident. We only put on steam.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA painter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, haven\xe2\x80\x99t I told you about it? I only told you the beginning then\r\nabout the murder of the old pawnbroker-woman. Well, the painter is mixed\r\nup in it...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I heard about that murder before and was rather interested in it...\r\npartly... for one reason.... I read about it in the papers, too....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLizaveta was murdered, too,\xe2\x80\x9d Nastasya blurted out, suddenly addressing\r\nRaskolnikov. She remained in the room all the time, standing by the door\r\nlistening.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLizaveta,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Raskolnikov hardly audibly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLizaveta, who sold old clothes. Didn\xe2\x80\x99t you know her? She used to come\r\nhere. She mended a shirt for you, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov turned to the wall where in the dirty, yellow paper he\r\npicked out one clumsy, white flower with brown lines on it and began\r\nexamining how many petals there were in it, how many scallops in the\r\npetals and how many lines on them. He felt his arms and legs as lifeless\r\nas though they had been cut off. He did not attempt to move, but stared\r\nobstinately at the flower.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what about the painter?\xe2\x80\x9d Zossimov interrupted Nastasya\xe2\x80\x99s chatter\r\nwith marked displeasure. She sighed and was silent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, he was accused of the murder,\xe2\x80\x9d Razumihin went on hotly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas there evidence against him then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEvidence, indeed! Evidence that was no evidence, and that\xe2\x80\x99s what we\r\nhave to prove. It was just as they pitched on those fellows, Koch and\r\nPestryakov, at first. Foo! how stupidly it\xe2\x80\x99s all done, it makes one\r\nsick, though it\xe2\x80\x99s not one\xe2\x80\x99s business! Pestryakov may be coming\r\nto-night.... By the way, Rodya, you\xe2\x80\x99ve heard about the business already;\r\nit happened before you were ill, the day before you fainted at the\r\npolice office while they were talking about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nZossimov looked curiously at Raskolnikov. He did not stir.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I say, Razumihin, I wonder at you. What a busybody you are!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Zossimov observed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaybe I am, but we will get him off anyway,\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Razumihin,\r\nbringing his fist down on the table. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the most offensive is not\r\ntheir lying--one can always forgive lying--lying is a delightful thing,\r\nfor it leads to truth--what is offensive is that they lie and worship\r\ntheir own lying.... I respect Porfiry, but... What threw them out at\r\nfirst? The door was locked, and when they came back with the porter\r\nit was open. So it followed that Koch and Pestryakov were the\r\nmurderers--that was their logic!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut don\xe2\x80\x99t excite yourself; they simply detained them, they could not\r\nhelp that.... And, by the way, I\xe2\x80\x99ve met that man Koch. He used to buy\r\nunredeemed pledges from the old woman? Eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, he is a swindler. He buys up bad debts, too. He makes a profession\r\nof it. But enough of him! Do you know what makes me angry? It\xe2\x80\x99s their\r\nsickening rotten, petrified routine.... And this case might be the means\r\nof introducing a new method. One can show from the psychological data\r\nalone how to get on the track of the real man. \xe2\x80\x98We have facts,\xe2\x80\x99 they\r\nsay. But facts are not everything--at least half the business lies in\r\nhow you interpret them!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you interpret them, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnyway, one can\xe2\x80\x99t hold one\xe2\x80\x99s tongue when one has a feeling, a tangible\r\nfeeling, that one might be a help if only.... Eh! Do you know the\r\ndetails of the case?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am waiting to hear about the painter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes! Well, here\xe2\x80\x99s the story. Early on the third day after the\r\nmurder, when they were still dandling Koch and Pestryakov--though they\r\naccounted for every step they took and it was as plain as a pikestaff--an\r\nunexpected fact turned up. A peasant called Dushkin, who keeps a\r\ndram-shop facing the house, brought to the police office a jeweller\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncase containing some gold ear-rings, and told a long rigamarole. \xe2\x80\x98The\r\nday before yesterday, just after eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock\xe2\x80\x99--mark the day and the\r\nhour!--\xe2\x80\x98a journeyman house-painter, Nikolay, who had been in to see me\r\nalready that day, brought me this box of gold ear-rings and stones, and\r\nasked me to give him two roubles for them. When I asked him where he got\r\nthem, he said that he picked them up in the street. I did not ask him\r\nanything more.\xe2\x80\x99 I am telling you Dushkin\xe2\x80\x99s story. \xe2\x80\x98I gave him a note\xe2\x80\x99--a\r\nrouble that is--\xe2\x80\x98for I thought if he did not pawn it with me he would\r\nwith another. It would all come to the same thing--he\xe2\x80\x99d spend it on\r\ndrink, so the thing had better be with me. The further you hide it\r\nthe quicker you will find it, and if anything turns up, if I hear any\r\nrumours, I\xe2\x80\x99ll take it to the police.\xe2\x80\x99 Of course, that\xe2\x80\x99s all taradiddle;\r\nhe lies like a horse, for I know this Dushkin, he is a pawnbroker and\r\na receiver of stolen goods, and he did not cheat Nikolay out of a\r\nthirty-rouble trinket in order to give it to the police. He was simply\r\nafraid. But no matter, to return to Dushkin\xe2\x80\x99s story. \xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ve known\r\nthis peasant, Nikolay Dementyev, from a child; he comes from the same\r\nprovince and district of Zara\xc3\xafsk, we are both Ryazan men. And though\r\nNikolay is not a drunkard, he drinks, and I knew he had a job in that\r\nhouse, painting work with Dmitri, who comes from the same village, too.\r\nAs soon as he got the rouble he changed it, had a couple of glasses,\r\ntook his change and went out. But I did not see Dmitri with him then.\r\nAnd the next day I heard that someone had murdered Alyona Ivanovna and\r\nher sister, Lizaveta Ivanovna, with an axe. I knew them, and I felt\r\nsuspicious about the ear-rings at once, for I knew the murdered woman\r\nlent money on pledges. I went to the house, and began to make careful\r\ninquiries without saying a word to anyone. First of all I asked, \xe2\x80\x9cIs\r\nNikolay here?\xe2\x80\x9d Dmitri told me that Nikolay had gone off on the spree; he\r\nhad come home at daybreak drunk, stayed in the house about ten minutes,\r\nand went out again. Dmitri didn\xe2\x80\x99t see him again and is finishing the\r\njob alone. And their job is on the same staircase as the murder, on\r\nthe second floor. When I heard all that I did not say a word to\r\nanyone\xe2\x80\x99--that\xe2\x80\x99s Dushkin\xe2\x80\x99s tale--\xe2\x80\x98but I found out what I could about\r\nthe murder, and went home feeling as suspicious as ever. And at eight\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock this morning\xe2\x80\x99--that was the third day, you understand--\xe2\x80\x98I saw\r\nNikolay coming in, not sober, though not to say very drunk--he could\r\nunderstand what was said to him. He sat down on the bench and did not\r\nspeak. There was only one stranger in the bar and a man I knew asleep\r\non a bench and our two boys. \xe2\x80\x9cHave you seen Dmitri?\xe2\x80\x9d said I. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, I\r\nhaven\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd you\xe2\x80\x99ve not been here either?\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cNot since the day\r\nbefore yesterday,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd where did you sleep last night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n \xe2\x80\x9cIn Peski, with the Kolomensky men.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cAnd where did you get those\r\near-rings?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked. \xe2\x80\x9cI found them in the street,\xe2\x80\x9d and the way he said\r\nit was a bit queer; he did not look at me. \xe2\x80\x9cDid you hear what happened\r\nthat very evening, at that very hour, on that same staircase?\xe2\x80\x9d said I.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cI had not heard,\xe2\x80\x9d and all the while he was listening,\r\nhis eyes were staring out of his head and he turned as white as chalk. I\r\ntold him all about it and he took his hat and began getting up. I wanted\r\nto keep him. \xe2\x80\x9cWait a bit, Nikolay,\xe2\x80\x9d said I, \xe2\x80\x9cwon\xe2\x80\x99t you have a drink?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n And I signed to the boy to hold the door, and I came out from behind the\r\nbar; but he darted out and down the street to the turning at a run.\r\nI have not seen him since. Then my doubts were at an end--it was his\r\ndoing, as clear as could be....\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should think so,\xe2\x80\x9d said Zossimov.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait! Hear the end. Of course they sought high and low for Nikolay;\r\nthey detained Dushkin and searched his house; Dmitri, too, was arrested;\r\nthe Kolomensky men also were turned inside out. And the day before\r\nyesterday they arrested Nikolay in a tavern at the end of the town. He\r\nhad gone there, taken the silver cross off his neck and asked for a dram\r\nfor it. They gave it to him. A few minutes afterwards the woman went\r\nto the cowshed, and through a crack in the wall she saw in the stable\r\nadjoining he had made a noose of his sash from the beam, stood on a\r\nblock of wood, and was trying to put his neck in the noose. The woman\r\nscreeched her hardest; people ran in. \xe2\x80\x98So that\xe2\x80\x99s what you are up to!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Take me,\xe2\x80\x99 he says, \xe2\x80\x98to such-and-such a police officer; I\xe2\x80\x99ll confess\r\neverything.\xe2\x80\x99 Well, they took him to that police station--that is\r\nhere--with a suitable escort. So they asked him this and that, how old\r\nhe is, \xe2\x80\x98twenty-two,\xe2\x80\x99 and so on. At the question, \xe2\x80\x98When you were working\r\nwith Dmitri, didn\xe2\x80\x99t you see anyone on the staircase at such-and-such a\r\ntime?\xe2\x80\x99--answer: \xe2\x80\x98To be sure folks may have gone up and down, but I did\r\nnot notice them.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98And didn\xe2\x80\x99t you hear anything, any noise, and so on?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\xe2\x80\x98We heard nothing special.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98And did you hear, Nikolay, that on the same\r\nday Widow So-and-so and her sister were murdered and robbed?\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98I\r\nnever knew a thing about it. The first I heard of it was from Afanasy\r\nPavlovitch the day before yesterday.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98And where did you find the\r\near-rings?\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98I found them on the pavement.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t you go to work\r\nwith Dmitri the other day?\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Because I was drinking.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98And where were\r\nyou drinking?\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Oh, in such-and-such a place.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Why did you run away\r\nfrom Dushkin\xe2\x80\x99s?\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Because I was awfully frightened.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98What were\r\nyou frightened of?\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98That I should be accused.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98How could you be\r\nfrightened, if you felt free from guilt?\xe2\x80\x99 Now, Zossimov, you may not\r\nbelieve me, that question was put literally in those words. I know it\r\nfor a fact, it was repeated to me exactly! What do you say to that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, anyway, there\xe2\x80\x99s the evidence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not talking of the evidence now, I am talking about that question,\r\nof their own idea of themselves. Well, so they squeezed and squeezed\r\nhim and he confessed: \xe2\x80\x98I did not find it in the street, but in the flat\r\nwhere I was painting with Dmitri.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98And how was that?\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Why, Dmitri and\r\nI were painting there all day, and we were just getting ready to go, and\r\nDmitri took a brush and painted my face, and he ran off and I after him.\r\nI ran after him, shouting my hardest, and at the bottom of the stairs I\r\nran right against the porter and some gentlemen--and how many gentlemen\r\nwere there I don\xe2\x80\x99t remember. And the porter swore at me, and the other\r\nporter swore, too, and the porter\xe2\x80\x99s wife came out, and swore at us, too;\r\nand a gentleman came into the entry with a lady, and he swore at us,\r\ntoo, for Dmitri and I lay right across the way. I got hold of Dmitri\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhair and knocked him down and began beating him. And Dmitri, too, caught\r\nme by the hair and began beating me. But we did it all not for temper\r\nbut in a friendly way, for sport. And then Dmitri escaped and ran into\r\nthe street, and I ran after him; but I did not catch him, and went back\r\nto the flat alone; I had to clear up my things. I began putting them\r\ntogether, expecting Dmitri to come, and there in the passage, in the\r\ncorner by the door, I stepped on the box. I saw it lying there wrapped\r\nup in paper. I took off the paper, saw some little hooks, undid them,\r\nand in the box were the ear-rings....\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBehind the door? Lying behind the door? Behind the door?\xe2\x80\x9d Raskolnikov\r\ncried suddenly, staring with a blank look of terror at Razumihin, and he\r\nslowly sat up on the sofa, leaning on his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes... why? What\xe2\x80\x99s the matter? What\xe2\x80\x99s wrong?\xe2\x80\x9d Razumihin, too, got up\r\nfrom his seat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing,\xe2\x80\x9d Raskolnikov answered faintly, turning to the wall. All were\r\nsilent for a while.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe must have waked from a dream,\xe2\x80\x9d Razumihin said at last, looking\r\ninquiringly at Zossimov. The latter slightly shook his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, go on,\xe2\x80\x9d said Zossimov. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat next?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat next? As soon as he saw the ear-rings, forgetting Dmitri and\r\neverything, he took up his cap and ran to Dushkin and, as we know, got\r\na rouble from him. He told a lie saying he found them in the street, and\r\nwent off drinking. He keeps repeating his old story about the murder:\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I know nothing of it, never heard of it till the day before yesterday.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And why didn\xe2\x80\x99t you come to the police till now?\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98I was frightened.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And why did you try to hang yourself?\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98From anxiety.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98What anxiety?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That I should be accused of it.\xe2\x80\x99 Well, that\xe2\x80\x99s the whole story. And now\r\nwhat do you suppose they deduced from that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, there\xe2\x80\x99s no supposing. There\xe2\x80\x99s a clue, such as it is, a fact. You\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have your painter set free?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow they\xe2\x80\x99ve simply taken him for the murderer. They haven\xe2\x80\x99t a shadow of\r\ndoubt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s nonsense. You are excited. But what about the ear-rings? You\r\nmust admit that, if on the very same day and hour ear-rings from the old\r\nwoman\xe2\x80\x99s box have come into Nikolay\xe2\x80\x99s hands, they must have come there\r\nsomehow. That\xe2\x80\x99s a good deal in such a case.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did they get there? How did they get there?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Razumihin.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can you, a doctor, whose duty it is to study man and who has more\r\nopportunity than anyone else for studying human nature--how can you fail\r\nto see the character of the man in the whole story? Don\xe2\x80\x99t you see at\r\nonce that the answers he has given in the examination are the holy\r\ntruth? They came into his hand precisely as he has told us--he stepped\r\non the box and picked it up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe holy truth! But didn\xe2\x80\x99t he own himself that he told a lie at first?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cListen to me, listen attentively. The porter and Koch and Pestryakov\r\nand the other porter and the wife of the first porter and the woman who\r\nwas sitting in the porter\xe2\x80\x99s lodge and the man Kryukov, who had just got\r\nout of a cab at that minute and went in at the entry with a lady on his\r\narm, that is eight or ten witnesses, agree that Nikolay had Dmitri on\r\nthe ground, was lying on him beating him, while Dmitri hung on to his\r\nhair, beating him, too. They lay right across the way, blocking the\r\nthoroughfare. They were sworn at on all sides while they \xe2\x80\x98like children\xe2\x80\x99\r\n(the very words of the witnesses) were falling over one another,\r\nsquealing, fighting and laughing with the funniest faces, and, chasing\r\none another like children, they ran into the street. Now take careful\r\nnote. The bodies upstairs were warm, you understand, warm when they\r\nfound them! If they, or Nikolay alone, had murdered them and broken open\r\nthe boxes, or simply taken part in the robbery, allow me to ask you one\r\nquestion: do their state of mind, their squeals and giggles and childish\r\nscuffling at the gate fit in with axes, bloodshed, fiendish cunning,\r\nrobbery? They\xe2\x80\x99d just killed them, not five or ten minutes before, for\r\nthe bodies were still warm, and at once, leaving the flat open, knowing\r\nthat people would go there at once, flinging away their booty, they\r\nrolled about like children, laughing and attracting general attention.\r\nAnd there are a dozen witnesses to swear to that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course it is strange! It\xe2\x80\x99s impossible, indeed, but...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, brother, no _buts_. And if the ear-rings being found in Nikolay\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhands at the very day and hour of the murder constitutes an important\r\npiece of circumstantial evidence against him--although the explanation\r\ngiven by him accounts for it, and therefore it does not tell seriously\r\nagainst him--one must take into consideration the facts which prove him\r\ninnocent, especially as they are facts that _cannot be denied_. And\r\ndo you suppose, from the character of our legal system, that they will\r\naccept, or that they are in a position to accept, this fact--resting\r\nsimply on a psychological impossibility--as irrefutable and conclusively\r\nbreaking down the circumstantial evidence for the prosecution? No, they\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t accept it, they certainly won\xe2\x80\x99t, because they found the jewel-case\r\nand the man tried to hang himself, \xe2\x80\x98which he could not have done if he\r\nhadn\xe2\x80\x99t felt guilty.\xe2\x80\x99 That\xe2\x80\x99s the point, that\xe2\x80\x99s what excites me, you must\r\nunderstand!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I see you are excited! Wait a bit. I forgot to ask you; what proof\r\nis there that the box came from the old woman?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s been proved,\xe2\x80\x9d said Razumihin with apparent reluctance, frowning.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKoch recognised the jewel-case and gave the name of the owner, who\r\nproved conclusively that it was his.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s bad. Now another point. Did anyone see Nikolay at the time\r\nthat Koch and Pestryakov were going upstairs at first, and is there no\r\nevidence about that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNobody did see him,\xe2\x80\x9d Razumihin answered with vexation. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nworst of it. Even Koch and Pestryakov did not notice them on their way\r\nupstairs, though, indeed, their evidence could not have been worth much.\r\nThey said they saw the flat was open, and that there must be work going\r\non in it, but they took no special notice and could not remember whether\r\nthere actually were men at work in it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHm!... So the only evidence for the defence is that they were beating\r\none another and laughing. That constitutes a strong presumption, but...\r\nHow do you explain the facts yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do I explain them? What is there to explain? It\xe2\x80\x99s clear. At any\r\nrate, the direction in which explanation is to be sought is clear, and\r\nthe jewel-case points to it. The real murderer dropped those ear-rings.\r\nThe murderer was upstairs, locked in, when Koch and Pestryakov knocked\r\nat the door. Koch, like an ass, did not stay at the door; so the\r\nmurderer popped out and ran down, too; for he had no other way of\r\nescape. He hid from Koch, Pestryakov and the porter in the flat when\r\nNikolay and Dmitri had just run out of it. He stopped there while the\r\nporter and others were going upstairs, waited till they were out of\r\nhearing, and then went calmly downstairs at the very minute when Dmitri\r\nand Nikolay ran out into the street and there was no one in the entry;\r\npossibly he was seen, but not noticed. There are lots of people going\r\nin and out. He must have dropped the ear-rings out of his pocket when\r\nhe stood behind the door, and did not notice he dropped them, because he\r\nhad other things to think of. The jewel-case is a conclusive proof that\r\nhe did stand there.... That\xe2\x80\x99s how I explain it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToo clever! No, my boy, you\xe2\x80\x99re too clever. That beats everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, why, why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, because everything fits too well... it\xe2\x80\x99s too melodramatic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA-ach!\xe2\x80\x9d Razumihin was exclaiming, but at that moment the door opened\r\nand a personage came in who was a stranger to all present.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER V\r\n\r\nThis was a gentleman no longer young, of a stiff and portly appearance,\r\nand a cautious and sour countenance. He began by stopping short in the\r\ndoorway, staring about him with offensive and undisguised astonishment,\r\nas though asking himself what sort of place he had come to.\r\nMistrustfully and with an affectation of being alarmed and almost\r\naffronted, he scanned Raskolnikov\xe2\x80\x99s low and narrow \xe2\x80\x9ccabin.\xe2\x80\x9d With the\r\nsame amazement he stared at Raskolnikov, who lay undressed, dishevelled,\r\nunwashed, on his miserable dirty sofa, looking fixedly at him. Then with\r\nthe same deliberation he scrutinised the uncouth, unkempt figure and\r\nunshaven face of Razumihin, who looked him boldly and inquiringly in the\r\nface without rising from his seat. A constrained silence lasted for a\r\ncouple of minutes, and then, as might be expected, some scene-shifting\r\ntook place. Reflecting, probably from certain fairly unmistakable signs,\r\nthat he would get nothing in this \xe2\x80\x9ccabin\xe2\x80\x9d by attempting to overawe them,\r\nthe gentleman softened somewhat, and civilly, though with some severity,\r\nemphasising every syllable of his question, addressed Zossimov:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRodion Romanovitch Raskolnikov, a student, or formerly a student?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nZossimov made a slight movement, and would have answered, had not\r\nRazumihin anticipated him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere he is lying on the sofa! What do you want?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis familiar \xe2\x80\x9cwhat do you want\xe2\x80\x9d seemed to cut the ground from the\r\nfeet of the pompous gentleman. He was turning to Razumihin, but checked\r\nhimself in time and turned to Zossimov again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is Raskolnikov,\xe2\x80\x9d mumbled Zossimov, nodding towards him. Then he\r\ngave a prolonged yawn, opening his mouth as wide as possible. Then he\r\nlazily put his hand into his waistcoat-pocket, pulled out a huge gold\r\nwatch in a round hunter\xe2\x80\x99s case, opened it, looked at it and as slowly\r\nand lazily proceeded to put it back.\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov himself lay without speaking, on his back, gazing\r\npersistently, though without understanding, at the stranger. Now that\r\nhis face was turned away from the strange flower on the paper, it\r\nwas extremely pale and wore a look of anguish, as though he had just\r\nundergone an agonising operation or just been taken from the rack. But\r\nthe new-comer gradually began to arouse his attention, then his wonder,\r\nthen suspicion and even alarm. When Zossimov said \xe2\x80\x9cThis is Raskolnikov\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n he jumped up quickly, sat on the sofa and with an almost defiant, but\r\nweak and breaking, voice articulated:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I am Raskolnikov! What do you want?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe visitor scrutinised him and pronounced impressively:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPyotr Petrovitch Luzhin. I believe I have reason to hope that my name\r\nis not wholly unknown to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Raskolnikov, who had expected something quite different, gazed\r\nblankly and dreamily at him, making no reply, as though he heard the\r\nname of Pyotr Petrovitch for the first time.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it possible that you can up to the present have received no\r\ninformation?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Pyotr Petrovitch, somewhat disconcerted.\r\n\r\nIn reply Raskolnikov sank languidly back on the pillow, put his hands\r\nbehind his head and gazed at the ceiling. A look of dismay came into\r\nLuzhin\xe2\x80\x99s face. Zossimov and Razumihin stared at him more inquisitively\r\nthan ever, and at last he showed unmistakable signs of embarrassment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had presumed and calculated,\xe2\x80\x9d he faltered, \xe2\x80\x9cthat a letter posted more\r\nthan ten days, if not a fortnight ago...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, why are you standing in the doorway?\xe2\x80\x9d Razumihin interrupted\r\nsuddenly. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you\xe2\x80\x99ve something to say, sit down. Nastasya and you are so\r\ncrowded. Nastasya, make room. Here\xe2\x80\x99s a chair, thread your way in!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe moved his chair back from the table, made a little space between the\r\ntable and his knees, and waited in a rather cramped position for the\r\nvisitor to \xe2\x80\x9cthread his way in.\xe2\x80\x9d The minute was so chosen that it was\r\nimpossible to refuse, and the visitor squeezed his way through, hurrying\r\nand stumbling. Reaching the chair, he sat down, looking suspiciously at\r\nRazumihin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo need to be nervous,\xe2\x80\x9d the latter blurted out. \xe2\x80\x9cRodya has been ill for\r\nthe last five days and delirious for three, but now he is recovering and\r\nhas got an appetite. This is his doctor, who has just had a look at him.\r\nI am a comrade of Rodya\xe2\x80\x99s, like him, formerly a student, and now I am\r\nnursing him; so don\xe2\x80\x99t you take any notice of us, but go on with your\r\nbusiness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you. But shall I not disturb the invalid by my presence and\r\nconversation?\xe2\x80\x9d Pyotr Petrovitch asked of Zossimov.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cN-no,\xe2\x80\x9d mumbled Zossimov; \xe2\x80\x9cyou may amuse him.\xe2\x80\x9d He yawned again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has been conscious a long time, since the morning,\xe2\x80\x9d went on\r\nRazumihin, whose familiarity seemed so much like unaffected good-nature\r\nthat Pyotr Petrovitch began to be more cheerful, partly, perhaps,\r\nbecause this shabby and impudent person had introduced himself as a\r\nstudent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour mamma,\xe2\x80\x9d began Luzhin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHm!\xe2\x80\x9d Razumihin cleared his throat loudly. Luzhin looked at him\r\ninquiringly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all right, go on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLuzhin shrugged his shoulders.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour mamma had commenced a letter to you while I was sojourning in\r\nher neighbourhood. On my arrival here I purposely allowed a few days to\r\nelapse before coming to see you, in order that I might be fully\r\nassured that you were in full possession of the tidings; but now, to my\r\nastonishment...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know, I know!\xe2\x80\x9d Raskolnikov cried suddenly with impatient vexation.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you are the _fianc\xc3\xa9_? I know, and that\xe2\x80\x99s enough!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was no doubt about Pyotr Petrovitch\xe2\x80\x99s being offended this time,\r\nbut he said nothing. He made a violent effort to understand what it all\r\nmeant. There was a moment\xe2\x80\x99s silence.\r\n\r\nMeanwhile Raskolnikov, who had turned a little towards him when he\r\nanswered, began suddenly staring at him again with marked curiosity, as\r\nthough he had not had a good look at him yet, or as though something\r\nnew had struck him; he rose from his pillow on purpose to stare at\r\nhim. There certainly was something peculiar in Pyotr Petrovitch\xe2\x80\x99s whole\r\nappearance, something which seemed to justify the title of \xe2\x80\x9cfianc\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x9d so\r\nunceremoniously applied to him. In the first place, it was evident, far\r\ntoo much so indeed, that Pyotr Petrovitch had made eager use of his few\r\ndays in the capital to get himself up and rig himself out in expectation\r\nof his betrothed--a perfectly innocent and permissible proceeding,\r\nindeed. Even his own, perhaps too complacent, consciousness of the\r\nagreeable improvement in his appearance might have been forgiven in such\r\ncircumstances, seeing that Pyotr Petrovitch had taken up the r\xc3\xb4le of\r\nfianc\xc3\xa9. All his clothes were fresh from the tailor\xe2\x80\x99s and were all\r\nright, except for being too new and too distinctly appropriate. Even\r\nthe stylish new round hat had the same significance. Pyotr Petrovitch\r\ntreated it too respectfully and held it too carefully in his hands. The\r\nexquisite pair of lavender gloves, real Louvain, told the same tale,\r\nif only from the fact of his not wearing them, but carrying them in\r\nhis hand for show. Light and youthful colours predominated in Pyotr\r\nPetrovitch\xe2\x80\x99s attire. He wore a charming summer jacket of a fawn shade,\r\nlight thin trousers, a waistcoat of the same, new and fine linen, a\r\ncravat of the lightest cambric with pink stripes on it, and the best\r\nof it was, this all suited Pyotr Petrovitch. His very fresh and even\r\nhandsome face looked younger than his forty-five years at all times.\r\nHis dark, mutton-chop whiskers made an agreeable setting on both sides,\r\ngrowing thickly upon his shining, clean-shaven chin. Even his hair,\r\ntouched here and there with grey, though it had been combed and curled\r\nat a hairdresser\xe2\x80\x99s, did not give him a stupid appearance, as curled hair\r\nusually does, by inevitably suggesting a German on his wedding-day.\r\nIf there really was something unpleasing and repulsive in his rather\r\ngood-looking and imposing countenance, it was due to quite other\r\ncauses. After scanning Mr. Luzhin unceremoniously, Raskolnikov smiled\r\nmalignantly, sank back on the pillow and stared at the ceiling as\r\nbefore.\r\n\r\nBut Mr. Luzhin hardened his heart and seemed to determine to take no\r\nnotice of their oddities.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI feel the greatest regret at finding you in this situation,\xe2\x80\x9d he began,\r\nagain breaking the silence with an effort. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I had been aware of your\r\nillness I should have come earlier. But you know what business is. I\r\nhave, too, a very important legal affair in the Senate, not to mention\r\nother preoccupations which you may well conjecture. I am expecting your\r\nmamma and sister any minute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov made a movement and seemed about to speak; his face showed\r\nsome excitement. Pyotr Petrovitch paused, waited, but as nothing\r\nfollowed, he went on:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c... Any minute. I have found a lodging for them on their arrival.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Raskolnikov weakly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery near here, in Bakaleyev\xe2\x80\x99s house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s in Voskresensky,\xe2\x80\x9d put in Razumihin. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are two storeys of\r\nrooms, let by a merchant called Yushin; I\xe2\x80\x99ve been there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, rooms...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA disgusting place--filthy, stinking and, what\xe2\x80\x99s more, of doubtful\r\ncharacter. Things have happened there, and there are all sorts of queer\r\npeople living there. And I went there about a scandalous business. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncheap, though...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could not, of course, find out so much about it, for I am a stranger\r\nin Petersburg myself,\xe2\x80\x9d Pyotr Petrovitch replied huffily. \xe2\x80\x9cHowever, the\r\ntwo rooms are exceedingly clean, and as it is for so short a time...\r\nI have already taken a permanent, that is, our future flat,\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\r\naddressing Raskolnikov, \xe2\x80\x9cand I am having it done up. And meanwhile I am\r\nmyself cramped for room in a lodging with my friend Andrey Semyonovitch\r\nLebeziatnikov, in the flat of Madame Lippevechsel; it was he who told me\r\nof Bakaleyev\xe2\x80\x99s house, too...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLebeziatnikov?\xe2\x80\x9d said Raskolnikov slowly, as if recalling something.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Andrey Semyonovitch Lebeziatnikov, a clerk in the Ministry. Do you\r\nknow him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes... no,\xe2\x80\x9d Raskolnikov answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me, I fancied so from your inquiry. I was once his guardian....\r\nA very nice young man and advanced. I like to meet young people: one\r\nlearns new things from them.\xe2\x80\x9d Luzhin looked round hopefully at them all.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Razumihin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the most serious and essential matters,\xe2\x80\x9d Pyotr Petrovitch replied,\r\nas though delighted at the question. \xe2\x80\x9cYou see, it\xe2\x80\x99s ten years since I\r\nvisited Petersburg. All the novelties, reforms, ideas have reached us in\r\nthe provinces, but to see it all more clearly one must be in Petersburg.\r\nAnd it\xe2\x80\x99s my notion that you observe and learn most by watching the\r\nyounger generation. And I confess I am delighted...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour question is a wide one. I may be mistaken, but I fancy I find\r\nclearer views, more, so to say, criticism, more practicality...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s true,\xe2\x80\x9d Zossimov let drop.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNonsense! There\xe2\x80\x99s no practicality.\xe2\x80\x9d Razumihin flew at him.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPracticality is a difficult thing to find; it does not drop down from\r\nheaven. And for the last two hundred years we have been divorced from\r\nall practical life. Ideas, if you like, are fermenting,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to\r\nPyotr Petrovitch, \xe2\x80\x9cand desire for good exists, though it\xe2\x80\x99s in a childish\r\nform, and honesty you may find, although there are crowds of brigands.\r\nAnyway, there\xe2\x80\x99s no practicality. Practicality goes well shod.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t agree with you,\xe2\x80\x9d Pyotr Petrovitch replied, with evident\r\nenjoyment. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course, people do get carried away and make mistakes,\r\nbut one must have indulgence; those mistakes are merely evidence of\r\nenthusiasm for the cause and of abnormal external environment. If little\r\nhas been done, the time has been but short; of means I will not speak.\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s my personal view, if you care to know, that something has been\r\naccomplished already. New valuable ideas, new valuable works are\r\ncirculating in the place of our old dreamy and romantic authors.\r\nLiterature is taking a maturer form, many injurious prejudices have been\r\nrooted up and turned into ridicule.... In a word, we have cut ourselves\r\noff irrevocably from the past, and that, to my thinking, is a great\r\nthing...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s learnt it by heart to show off!\xe2\x80\x9d Raskolnikov pronounced suddenly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Pyotr Petrovitch, not catching his words; but he received\r\nno reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all true,\xe2\x80\x9d Zossimov hastened to interpose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it so?\xe2\x80\x9d Pyotr Petrovitch went on, glancing affably at Zossimov.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must admit,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, addressing Razumihin with a shade of\r\ntriumph and superciliousness--he almost added \xe2\x80\x9cyoung man\xe2\x80\x9d--\xe2\x80\x9cthat there\r\nis an advance, or, as they say now, progress in the name of science and\r\neconomic truth...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA commonplace.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, not a commonplace! Hitherto, for instance, if I were told, \xe2\x80\x98love\r\nthy neighbour,\xe2\x80\x99 what came of it?\xe2\x80\x9d Pyotr Petrovitch went on, perhaps with\r\nexcessive haste. \xe2\x80\x9cIt came to my tearing my coat in half to share with my\r\nneighbour and we both were left half naked. As a Russian proverb has\r\nit, \xe2\x80\x98Catch several hares and you won\xe2\x80\x99t catch one.\xe2\x80\x99 Science now tells\r\nus, love yourself before all men, for everything in the world rests on\r\nself-interest. You love yourself and manage your own affairs properly\r\nand your coat remains whole. Economic truth adds that the better private\r\naffairs are organised in society--the more whole coats, so to say--the\r\nfirmer are its foundations and the better is the common welfare\r\norganised too. Therefore, in acquiring wealth solely and exclusively for\r\nmyself, I am acquiring, so to speak, for all, and helping to bring to\r\npass my neighbour\xe2\x80\x99s getting a little more than a torn coat; and that not\r\nfrom private, personal liberality, but as a consequence of the general\r\nadvance. The idea is simple, but unhappily it has been a long time\r\nreaching us, being hindered by idealism and sentimentality. And yet it\r\nwould seem to want very little wit to perceive it...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me, I\xe2\x80\x99ve very little wit myself,\xe2\x80\x9d Razumihin cut in sharply,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand so let us drop it. I began this discussion with an object, but I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\ngrown so sick during the last three years of this chattering to amuse\r\noneself, of this incessant flow of commonplaces, always the same, that,\r\nby Jove, I blush even when other people talk like that. You are in a\r\nhurry, no doubt, to exhibit your acquirements; and I don\xe2\x80\x99t blame you,\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s quite pardonable. I only wanted to find out what sort of man you\r\nare, for so many unscrupulous people have got hold of the progressive\r\ncause of late and have so distorted in their own interests everything\r\nthey touched, that the whole cause has been dragged in the mire. That\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nenough!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luzhin, affronted, and speaking with excessive\r\ndignity. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean to suggest so unceremoniously that I too...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my dear sir... how could I?... Come, that\xe2\x80\x99s enough,\xe2\x80\x9d Razumihin\r\nconcluded, and he turned abruptly to Zossimov to continue their previous\r\nconversation.\r\n\r\nPyotr Petrovitch had the good sense to accept the disavowal. He made up\r\nhis mind to take leave in another minute or two.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI trust our acquaintance,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, addressing Raskolnikov, \xe2\x80\x9cmay, upon\r\nyour recovery and in view of the circumstances of which you are aware,\r\nbecome closer... Above all, I hope for your return to health...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov did not even turn his head. Pyotr Petrovitch began getting\r\nup from his chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne of her customers must have killed her,\xe2\x80\x9d Zossimov declared\r\npositively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a doubt of it,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Razumihin. \xe2\x80\x9cPorfiry doesn\xe2\x80\x99t give his\r\nopinion, but is examining all who have left pledges with her there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExamining them?\xe2\x80\x9d Raskolnikov asked aloud.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. What then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow does he get hold of them?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Zossimov.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKoch has given the names of some of them, other names are on the\r\nwrappers of the pledges and some have come forward of themselves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt must have been a cunning and practised ruffian! The boldness of it!\r\nThe coolness!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s just what it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d interposed Razumihin. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what throws\r\nyou all off the scent. But I maintain that he is not cunning, not\r\npractised, and probably this was his first crime! The supposition that\r\nit was a calculated crime and a cunning criminal doesn\xe2\x80\x99t work. Suppose\r\nhim to have been inexperienced, and it\xe2\x80\x99s clear that it was only a chance\r\nthat saved him--and chance may do anything. Why, he did not foresee\r\nobstacles, perhaps! And how did he set to work? He took jewels worth\r\nten or twenty roubles, stuffing his pockets with them, ransacked the\r\nold woman\xe2\x80\x99s trunks, her rags--and they found fifteen hundred roubles,\r\nbesides notes, in a box in the top drawer of the chest! He did not know\r\nhow to rob; he could only murder. It was his first crime, I assure you,\r\nhis first crime; he lost his head. And he got off more by luck than good\r\ncounsel!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are talking of the murder of the old pawnbroker, I believe?\xe2\x80\x9d Pyotr\r\nPetrovitch put in, addressing Zossimov. He was standing, hat and gloves\r\nin hand, but before departing he felt disposed to throw off a few more\r\nintellectual phrases. He was evidently anxious to make a favourable\r\nimpression and his vanity overcame his prudence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. You\xe2\x80\x99ve heard of it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, being in the neighbourhood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know the details?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t say that; but another circumstance interests me in the\r\ncase--the whole question, so to say. Not to speak of the fact that crime\r\nhas been greatly on the increase among the lower classes during the last\r\nfive years, not to speak of the cases of robbery and arson everywhere,\r\nwhat strikes me as the strangest thing is that in the higher classes,\r\ntoo, crime is increasing proportionately. In one place one hears of a\r\nstudent\xe2\x80\x99s robbing the mail on the high road; in another place people of\r\ngood social position forge false banknotes; in Moscow of late a whole\r\ngang has been captured who used to forge lottery tickets, and one of\r\nthe ringleaders was a lecturer in universal history; then our secretary\r\nabroad was murdered from some obscure motive of gain.... And if this old\r\nwoman, the pawnbroker, has been murdered by someone of a higher class\r\nin society--for peasants don\xe2\x80\x99t pawn gold trinkets--how are we to explain\r\nthis demoralisation of the civilised part of our society?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are many economic changes,\xe2\x80\x9d put in Zossimov.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow are we to explain it?\xe2\x80\x9d Razumihin caught him up. \xe2\x80\x9cIt might be\r\nexplained by our inveterate impracticality.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat answer had your lecturer in Moscow to make to the question why he\r\nwas forging notes? \xe2\x80\x98Everybody is getting rich one way or another, so I\r\nwant to make haste to get rich too.\xe2\x80\x99 I don\xe2\x80\x99t remember the exact words,\r\nbut the upshot was that he wants money for nothing, without waiting or\r\nworking! We\xe2\x80\x99ve grown used to having everything ready-made, to walking\r\non crutches, to having our food chewed for us. Then the great hour\r\nstruck,[*] and every man showed himself in his true colours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n     [*] The emancipation of the serfs in 1861 is meant.\r\n     --TRANSLATOR\xe2\x80\x99S NOTE.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut morality? And so to speak, principles...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why do you worry about it?\xe2\x80\x9d Raskolnikov interposed suddenly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nin accordance with your theory!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn accordance with my theory?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, carry out logically the theory you were advocating just now, and\r\nit follows that people may be killed...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUpon my word!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Luzhin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, that\xe2\x80\x99s not so,\xe2\x80\x9d put in Zossimov.\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov lay with a white face and twitching upper lip, breathing\r\npainfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a measure in all things,\xe2\x80\x9d Luzhin went on superciliously.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEconomic ideas are not an incitement to murder, and one has but to\r\nsuppose...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd is it true,\xe2\x80\x9d Raskolnikov interposed once more suddenly, again in a\r\nvoice quivering with fury and delight in insulting him, \xe2\x80\x9cis it true that\r\nyou told your _fianc\xc3\xa9e_... within an hour of her acceptance, that what\r\npleased you most... was that she was a beggar... because it was better\r\nto raise a wife from poverty, so that you may have complete control over\r\nher, and reproach her with your being her benefactor?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUpon my word,\xe2\x80\x9d Luzhin cried wrathfully and irritably, crimson with\r\nconfusion, \xe2\x80\x9cto distort my words in this way! Excuse me, allow me to\r\nassure you that the report which has reached you, or rather, let me say,\r\nhas been conveyed to you, has no foundation in truth, and I... suspect\r\nwho... in a word... this arrow... in a word, your mamma... She seemed\r\nto me in other things, with all her excellent qualities, of a somewhat\r\nhigh-flown and romantic way of thinking.... But I was a thousand miles\r\nfrom supposing that she would misunderstand and misrepresent things in\r\nso fanciful a way.... And indeed... indeed...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI tell you what,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Raskolnikov, raising himself on his pillow and\r\nfixing his piercing, glittering eyes upon him, \xe2\x80\x9cI tell you what.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d Luzhin stood still, waiting with a defiant and offended face.\r\nSilence lasted for some seconds.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, if ever again... you dare to mention a single word... about my\r\nmother... I shall send you flying downstairs!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with you?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Razumihin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo that\xe2\x80\x99s how it is?\xe2\x80\x9d Luzhin turned pale and bit his lip. \xe2\x80\x9cLet me tell\r\nyou, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d he began deliberately, doing his utmost to restrain himself\r\nbut breathing hard, \xe2\x80\x9cat the first moment I saw you you were ill-disposed\r\nto me, but I remained here on purpose to find out more. I could forgive\r\na great deal in a sick man and a connection, but you... never after\r\nthis...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not ill,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Raskolnikov.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo much the worse...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo to hell!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Luzhin was already leaving without finishing his speech, squeezing\r\nbetween the table and the chair; Razumihin got up this time to let him\r\npass. Without glancing at anyone, and not even nodding to Zossimov, who\r\nhad for some time been making signs to him to let the sick man alone,\r\nhe went out, lifting his hat to the level of his shoulders to avoid\r\ncrushing it as he stooped to go out of the door. And even the curve of\r\nhis spine was expressive of the horrible insult he had received.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow could you--how could you!\xe2\x80\x9d Razumihin said, shaking his head in\r\nperplexity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me alone--let me alone all of you!\xe2\x80\x9d Raskolnikov cried in a frenzy.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you ever leave off tormenting me? I am not afraid of you! I am\r\nnot afraid of anyone, anyone now! Get away from me! I want to be alone,\r\nalone, alone!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome along,\xe2\x80\x9d said Zossimov, nodding to Razumihin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we can\xe2\x80\x99t leave him like this!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome along,\xe2\x80\x9d Zossimov repeated insistently, and he went out. Razumihin\r\nthought a minute and ran to overtake him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt might be worse not to obey him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Zossimov on the stairs. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\r\nmustn\xe2\x80\x99t be irritated.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf only he could get some favourable shock, that\xe2\x80\x99s what would do it! At\r\nfirst he was better.... You know he has got something on his mind! Some\r\nfixed idea weighing on him.... I am very much afraid so; he must have!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps it\xe2\x80\x99s that gentleman, Pyotr Petrovitch. From his conversation\r\nI gather he is going to marry his sister, and that he had received a\r\nletter about it just before his illness....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, confound the man! he may have upset the case altogether. But have\r\nyou noticed, he takes no interest in anything, he does not respond to\r\nanything except one point on which he seems excited--that\xe2\x80\x99s the murder?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d Razumihin agreed, \xe2\x80\x9cI noticed that, too. He is interested,\r\nfrightened. It gave him a shock on the day he was ill in the police\r\noffice; he fainted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell me more about that this evening and I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you something\r\nafterwards. He interests me very much! In half an hour I\xe2\x80\x99ll go and see\r\nhim again.... There\xe2\x80\x99ll be no inflammation though.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks! And I\xe2\x80\x99ll wait with Pashenka meantime and will keep watch on him\r\nthrough Nastasya....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov, left alone, looked with impatience and misery at Nastasya,\r\nbut she still lingered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWon\xe2\x80\x99t you have some tea now?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLater! I am sleepy! Leave me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe turned abruptly to the wall; Nastasya went out.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VI\r\n\r\nBut as soon as she went out, he got up, latched the door, undid the\r\nparcel which Razumihin had brought in that evening and had tied up again\r\nand began dressing. Strange to say, he seemed immediately to have become\r\nperfectly calm; not a trace of his recent delirium nor of the panic\r\nfear that had haunted him of late. It was the first moment of a strange\r\nsudden calm. His movements were precise and definite; a firm purpose was\r\nevident in them. \xe2\x80\x9cTo-day, to-day,\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered to himself. He understood\r\nthat he was still weak, but his intense spiritual concentration gave him\r\nstrength and self-confidence. He hoped, moreover, that he would not\r\nfall down in the street. When he had dressed in entirely new clothes, he\r\nlooked at the money lying on the table, and after a moment\xe2\x80\x99s thought\r\nput it in his pocket. It was twenty-five roubles. He took also all the\r\ncopper change from the ten roubles spent by Razumihin on the clothes.\r\nThen he softly unlatched the door, went out, slipped downstairs and\r\nglanced in at the open kitchen door. Nastasya was standing with her back\r\nto him, blowing up the landlady\xe2\x80\x99s samovar. She heard nothing. Who would\r\nhave dreamed of his going out, indeed? A minute later he was in the\r\nstreet.\r\n\r\nIt was nearly eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock, the sun was setting. It was as stifling as\r\nbefore, but he eagerly drank in the stinking, dusty town air. His head\r\nfelt rather dizzy; a sort of savage energy gleamed suddenly in his\r\nfeverish eyes and his wasted, pale and yellow face. He did not know and\r\ndid not think where he was going, he had one thought only: \xe2\x80\x9cthat all\r\n_this_ must be ended to-day, once for all, immediately; that he would\r\nnot return home without it, because he _would not go on living like\r\nthat_.\xe2\x80\x9d How, with what to make an end? He had not an idea about it,\r\nhe did not even want to think of it. He drove away thought; thought\r\ntortured him. All he knew, all he felt was that everything must be\r\nchanged \xe2\x80\x9cone way or another,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated with desperate and immovable\r\nself-confidence and determination.\r\n\r\nFrom old habit he took his usual walk in the direction of the Hay\r\nMarket. A dark-haired young man with a barrel organ was standing in\r\nthe road in front of a little general shop and was grinding out a very\r\nsentimental song. He was accompanying a girl of fifteen, who stood\r\non the pavement in front of him. She was dressed up in a crinoline, a\r\nmantle and a straw hat with a flame-coloured feather in it, all very\r\nold and shabby. In a strong and rather agreeable voice, cracked and\r\ncoarsened by street singing, she sang in hope of getting a copper from\r\nthe shop. Raskolnikov joined two or three listeners, took out a five\r\ncopeck piece and put it in the girl\xe2\x80\x99s hand. She broke off abruptly on a\r\nsentimental high note, shouted sharply to the organ grinder \xe2\x80\x9cCome on,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n and both moved on to the next shop.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you like street music?\xe2\x80\x9d said Raskolnikov, addressing a middle-aged\r\nman standing idly by him. The man looked at him, startled and wondering.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI love to hear singing to a street organ,\xe2\x80\x9d said Raskolnikov, and his\r\nmanner seemed strangely out of keeping with the subject--\xe2\x80\x9cI like it\r\non cold, dark, damp autumn evenings--they must be damp--when all the\r\npassers-by have pale green, sickly faces, or better still when wet\r\nsnow is falling straight down, when there\xe2\x80\x99s no wind--you know what I\r\nmean?--and the street lamps shine through it...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know.... Excuse me...\xe2\x80\x9d muttered the stranger, frightened by the\r\nquestion and Raskolnikov\xe2\x80\x99s strange manner, and he crossed over to the\r\nother side of the street.\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov walked straight on and came out at the corner of the Hay\r\nMarket, where the huckster and his wife had talked with Lizaveta; but\r\nthey were not there now. Recognising the place, he stopped, looked round\r\nand addressed a young fellow in a red shirt who stood gaping before a\r\ncorn chandler\xe2\x80\x99s shop.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t there a man who keeps a booth with his wife at this corner?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll sorts of people keep booths here,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the young man, glancing\r\nsuperciliously at Raskolnikov.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s his name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat he was christened.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAren\xe2\x80\x99t you a Zara\xc3\xafsky man, too? Which province?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young man looked at Raskolnikov again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s not a province, your excellency, but a district. Graciously\r\nforgive me, your excellency!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that a tavern at the top there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it\xe2\x80\x99s an eating-house and there\xe2\x80\x99s a billiard-room and you\xe2\x80\x99ll find\r\nprincesses there too.... La-la!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov crossed the square. In that corner there was a dense crowd\r\nof peasants. He pushed his way into the thickest part of it, looking\r\nat the faces. He felt an unaccountable inclination to enter into\r\nconversation with people. But the peasants took no notice of him; they\r\nwere all shouting in groups together. He stood and thought a little and\r\ntook a turning to the right in the direction of V.\r\n\r\nHe had often crossed that little street which turns at an angle, leading\r\nfrom the market-place to Sadovy Street. Of late he had often felt drawn\r\nto wander about this district, when he felt depressed, that he might\r\nfeel more so.\r\n\r\nNow he walked along, thinking of nothing. At that point there is a great\r\nblock of buildings, entirely let out in dram shops and eating-houses;\r\nwomen were continually running in and out, bare-headed and in their\r\nindoor clothes. Here and there they gathered in groups, on the pavement,\r\nespecially about the entrances to various festive establishments in\r\nthe lower storeys. From one of these a loud din, sounds of singing, the\r\ntinkling of a guitar and shouts of merriment, floated into the street.\r\nA crowd of women were thronging round the door; some were sitting on the\r\nsteps, others on the pavement, others were standing talking. A drunken\r\nsoldier, smoking a cigarette, was walking near them in the road,\r\nswearing; he seemed to be trying to find his way somewhere, but had\r\nforgotten where. One beggar was quarrelling with another, and a man dead\r\ndrunk was lying right across the road. Raskolnikov joined the throng of\r\nwomen, who were talking in husky voices. They were bare-headed and wore\r\ncotton dresses and goatskin shoes. There were women of forty and some\r\nnot more than seventeen; almost all had blackened eyes.\r\n\r\nHe felt strangely attracted by the singing and all the noise and\r\nuproar in the saloon below.... someone could be heard within dancing\r\nfrantically, marking time with his heels to the sounds of the guitar\r\nand of a thin falsetto voice singing a jaunty air. He listened intently,\r\ngloomily and dreamily, bending down at the entrance and peeping\r\ninquisitively in from the pavement.\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cOh, my handsome soldier\r\n   Don\xe2\x80\x99t beat me for nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\ntrilled the thin voice of the singer. Raskolnikov felt a great desire to\r\nmake out what he was singing, as though everything depended on that.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I go in?\xe2\x80\x9d he thought. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are laughing. From drink. Shall I get\r\ndrunk?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWon\xe2\x80\x99t you come in?\xe2\x80\x9d one of the women asked him. Her voice was\r\nstill musical and less thick than the others, she was young and not\r\nrepulsive--the only one of the group.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, she\xe2\x80\x99s pretty,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, drawing himself up and looking at her.\r\n\r\nShe smiled, much pleased at the compliment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re very nice looking yourself,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t he thin though!\xe2\x80\x9d observed another woman in a deep bass. \xe2\x80\x9cHave you\r\njust come out of a hospital?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re all generals\xe2\x80\x99 daughters, it seems, but they have all snub\r\nnoses,\xe2\x80\x9d interposed a tipsy peasant with a sly smile on his face, wearing\r\na loose coat. \xe2\x80\x9cSee how jolly they are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo along with you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll go, sweetie!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd he darted down into the saloon below. Raskolnikov moved on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d the girl shouted after him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe hesitated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll always be pleased to spend an hour with you, kind gentleman, but\r\nnow I feel shy. Give me six copecks for a drink, there\xe2\x80\x99s a nice young\r\nman!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov gave her what came first--fifteen copecks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, what a good-natured gentleman!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s your name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAsk for Duclida.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that\xe2\x80\x99s too much,\xe2\x80\x9d one of the women observed, shaking her head\r\nat Duclida. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know how you can ask like that. I believe I should\r\ndrop with shame....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov looked curiously at the speaker. She was a pock-marked wench\r\nof thirty, covered with bruises, with her upper lip swollen. She made\r\nher criticism quietly and earnestly. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere is it,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Raskolnikov.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is it I\xe2\x80\x99ve read that someone condemned to death says or thinks,\r\nan hour before his death, that if he had to live on some high rock,\r\non such a narrow ledge that he\xe2\x80\x99d only room to stand, and the ocean,\r\neverlasting darkness, everlasting solitude, everlasting tempest around\r\nhim, if he had to remain standing on a square yard of space all his\r\nlife, a thousand years, eternity, it were better to live so than to die\r\nat once! And vile\r\nis he who calls him vile for that,\xe2\x80\x9d he added a moment later.\r\n\r\nHe went into another street. \xe2\x80\x9cBah, the Palais de Cristal! Razumihin\r\nwas just talking of the Palais de Cristal. But what on earth was it\r\nI wanted? Yes, the newspapers.... Zossimov said he\xe2\x80\x99d read it in the\r\npapers. Raskolnikov\r\nfancied that Zametov was one of them, but he could not be sure at that\r\ndistance. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat if it is?\xe2\x80\x9d he thought.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you have vodka?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the waiter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive me some tea and bring me the papers, the old ones for the last\r\nfive days, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll give you something.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir, here\xe2\x80\x99s to-day\xe2\x80\x99s. No vodka?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old newspapers and the tea were brought. Raskolnikov sat down and\r\nbegan to look through them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, damn... these are the items of intelligence. An accident on a\r\nstaircase, spontaneous combustion of a shopkeeper from alcohol, a fire\r\nin Peski... a fire in the Petersburg quarter... another fire in the\r\nPetersburg quarter... and another fire in the Petersburg quarter....\r\nAh, here it is!\xe2\x80\x9d He found at last what he was seeking and began to\r\nread it. Suddenly someone\r\nsat down beside him at his table. He looked up, it was the head clerk\r\nZametov, looking just the same, with the rings on his fingers and the\r\nwatch-chain, with the curly, black hair, parted and pomaded, with the\r\nsmart waistcoat, rather shabby coat and doubtful linen. He says\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve been with him to Luise Ivanovna\xe2\x80\x99s--you know, the woman you tried\r\nto befriend, for whom you winked to the Explosive Lieutenant and he\r\nwould not understand. How could he fail to\r\nunderstand--it was quite clear, wasn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a hot head he is!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe explosive one?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, your friend Razumihin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must have a jolly life, Mr. Zametov; entrance free to the most\r\nagreeable places. He seemed to have completely forgotten\r\nZametov. The silence lasted for some time.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you drink your tea? He went on drinking tea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere have been a great many of these crimes lately,\xe2\x80\x9d said Zametov.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly the other day I read in the _Moscow News_ that a whole gang of\r\nfalse coiners had been caught in Moscow. It was a regular society. They\r\nused to forge tickets!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but it was a long time ago! I read about it a month ago,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Raskolnikov answered calmly. \xe2\x80\x9cSo you consider them criminals?\xe2\x80\x9d he added,\r\nsmiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course they are criminals.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey? They are children, simpletons, not criminals! Why, half a hundred\r\npeople meeting for such an object--what an idea! Three would be too\r\nmany, and then they want to have more faith in one another than in\r\nthemselves! One has only to blab in his cups and it all collapses.\r\nSimpletons! They engaged untrustworthy people to change the notes--what\r\na thing to trust to a casual stranger! Well, let us suppose that these\r\nsimpletons succeed and each makes a million, and what follows for the\r\nrest of their lives? Each is dependent on the others for the rest of his\r\nlife! Better hang oneself at once! And they did not know how to change\r\nthe notes either; the man who changed the notes took five thousand\r\nroubles, and his hands trembled. He counted the first four thousand,\r\nbut did not count the fifth thousand--he was in such a hurry to get the\r\nmoney into his pocket and run away. Of course he roused suspicion. And\r\nthe whole thing came to a crash through one fool! Is it possible?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat his hands trembled?\xe2\x80\x9d observed Zametov, \xe2\x80\x9cyes, that\xe2\x80\x99s quite\r\npossible. That, I feel quite sure, is possible. Sometimes one can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nstand things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t stand that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, could you stand it then? No, I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t. For the sake of a hundred\r\nroubles to face such a terrible experience? To go with false notes\r\ninto a bank where it\xe2\x80\x99s their business to spot that sort of thing! No, I\r\nshould not have the face to do it. Would you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov had an intense desire again \xe2\x80\x9cto put his tongue out.\xe2\x80\x9d Shivers\r\nkept running down his spine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should do it quite differently,\xe2\x80\x9d Raskolnikov began. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is how I\r\nwould change the notes: I\xe2\x80\x99d count the first thousand three or four times\r\nbackwards and forwards, looking at every note and then I\xe2\x80\x99d set to the\r\nsecond thousand; I\xe2\x80\x99d count that half-way through and then hold some\r\nfifty-rouble note to the light, then turn it, then hold it to the light\r\nagain--to see whether it was a good one. \xe2\x80\x98I am afraid,\xe2\x80\x99 I would say, \xe2\x80\x98a\r\nrelation of mine lost twenty-five roubles the other day through a\r\nfalse note,\xe2\x80\x99 and then I\xe2\x80\x99d tell them the whole story. And after I began\r\ncounting the third, \xe2\x80\x98No, excuse me,\xe2\x80\x99 I would say, \xe2\x80\x98I fancy I made a\r\nmistake in the seventh hundred in that second thousand, I am not sure.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nAnd so I would give up the third thousand and go back to the second and\r\nso on to the end. And when I had finished, I\xe2\x80\x99d pick out one from the\r\nfifth and one from the second thousand and take them again to the light\r\nand ask again, \xe2\x80\x98Change them, please,\xe2\x80\x99 and put the clerk into such a stew\r\nthat he would not know how to get rid of me. When I\xe2\x80\x99d finished and had\r\ngone out, I\xe2\x80\x99d come back, \xe2\x80\x98No, excuse me,\xe2\x80\x99 and ask for some explanation.\r\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s how I\xe2\x80\x99d do it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFoo! what terrible things you say!\xe2\x80\x9d said Zametov, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cBut all\r\nthat is only talk. I dare say when it came to deeds you\xe2\x80\x99d make a slip.\r\nI believe that even a practised, desperate man cannot always reckon on\r\nhimself, much less you and I. To take an example near home--that old\r\nwoman murdered in our district. The murderer seems to have been a\r\ndesperate fellow, he risked everything in open daylight, was saved by\r\na miracle--but his hands shook, too. He did not succeed in robbing the\r\nplace, he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t stand it. That was clear from the...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov seemed offended.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClear? Why don\xe2\x80\x99t you catch him then?\xe2\x80\x9d he cried, maliciously gibing at\r\nZametov.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, they will catch him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho? You? Do you suppose you could catch him? You\xe2\x80\x99ve a tough job! A\r\ngreat point for you is whether a man is spending money or not. If he had\r\nno money and suddenly begins spending, he must be the man. So that any\r\nchild can mislead you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe fact is they always do that, though,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Zametov. \xe2\x80\x9cA man will\r\ncommit a clever murder at the risk of his life and then at once he goes\r\ndrinking in a tavern. They are caught spending money, they are not all\r\nas cunning as you are. You wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t go to a tavern, of course?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov frowned and looked steadily at Zametov.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou seem to enjoy the subject and would like to know how I should\r\nbehave in that case, too?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked with displeasure.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to,\xe2\x80\x9d Zametov answered firmly and seriously. Somewhat too\r\nmuch earnestness began to appear in his words and looks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery much?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery much!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right then. This is how I should behave,\xe2\x80\x9d Raskolnikov began, again\r\nbringing his face close to Zametov\xe2\x80\x99s, again staring at him and speaking\r\nin a whisper, so that the latter positively shuddered. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is what\r\nI should have done. I should have taken the money and jewels, I should\r\nhave walked out of there and have gone straight to some deserted place\r\nwith fences round it and scarcely anyone to be seen, some kitchen garden\r\nor place of that sort. I should have looked out beforehand some stone\r\nweighing a hundredweight or more which had been lying in the corner from\r\nthe time the house was built. I would lift that stone--there would sure\r\nto be a hollow under it, and I would put the jewels and money in that\r\nhole. Then I\xe2\x80\x99d roll the stone back so that it would look as before,\r\nwould press it down with my foot and walk away. And for a year or two,\r\nthree maybe, I would not touch it. And, well, they could search! There\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nbe no trace.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are a madman,\xe2\x80\x9d said Zametov, and for some reason he too spoke in a\r\nwhisper, and moved away from Raskolnikov, whose eyes were glittering. He\r\nhad turned fearfully pale and his upper lip was twitching and quivering.\r\nHe bent down as close as possible to Zametov, and his lips began to move\r\nwithout uttering a word. This lasted for half a minute; he knew what he\r\nwas doing, but could not restrain himself. The terrible word trembled on\r\nhis lips, like the latch on that door; in another moment it will break\r\nout, in another moment he will let it go, he will speak out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what if it was I who murdered the old woman and Lizaveta?\xe2\x80\x9d he said\r\nsuddenly and--realised what he had done.\r\n\r\nZametov looked wildly at him and turned white as the tablecloth. His\r\nface wore a contorted smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut is it possible?\xe2\x80\x9d he brought out faintly. Raskolnikov looked\r\nwrathfully at him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOwn up that you believed it, yes, you did?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a bit of it, I believe it less than ever now,\xe2\x80\x9d Zametov cried\r\nhastily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve caught my cock-sparrow! So you did believe it before, if now you\r\nbelieve less than ever?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Zametov, obviously embarrassed. \xe2\x80\x9cHave you been\r\nfrightening me so as to lead up to this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t believe it then? What were you talking about behind my\r\nback when I went out of the police-office? And why did the explosive\r\nlieutenant question me after I fainted? Hey, there,\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted to the\r\nwaiter, getting up and taking his cap, \xe2\x80\x9chow much?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThirty copecks,\xe2\x80\x9d the latter replied, running up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd there is twenty copecks for vodka. See what a lot of money!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nheld out his shaking hand to Zametov with notes in it. \xe2\x80\x9cRed notes and\r\nblue, twenty-five roubles. Where did I get them? And where did my new\r\nclothes come from? You know I had not a copeck. You\xe2\x80\x99ve cross-examined my\r\nlandlady, I\xe2\x80\x99ll be bound.... Well, that\xe2\x80\x99s enough! _Assez caus\xc3\xa9!_ Till we\r\nmeet again!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe went out, trembling all over from a sort of wild hysterical\r\nsensation, in which there was an element of insufferable rapture. Yet he\r\nwas gloomy and terribly tired. His face was twisted as after a fit.\r\nHis fatigue increased rapidly. Any shock, any irritating sensation\r\nstimulated and revived his energies at once, but his strength failed as\r\nquickly when the stimulus was removed.\r\n\r\nZametov, left alone, sat for a long time in the same place, plunged in\r\nthought. Raskolnikov had unwittingly worked a revolution in his brain on\r\na certain point and had made up his mind for him conclusively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIlya Petrovitch is a blockhead,\xe2\x80\x9d he decided.\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov had hardly opened the door of the restaurant when he\r\nstumbled against Razumihin on the steps. They did not see each other\r\ntill they almost knocked against each other. For a moment they stood\r\nlooking each other up and down. Razumihin was greatly astounded, then\r\nanger, real anger gleamed fiercely in his eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo here you are!\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted at the top of his voice--\xe2\x80\x9cyou ran away\r\nfrom your bed! And here I\xe2\x80\x99ve been looking for you under the sofa! We\r\nwent up to the garret. I almost beat Nastasya on your account. And here\r\nhe is after all. Rodya! What is the meaning of it? Tell me the whole\r\ntruth! Confess! Do you hear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt means that I\xe2\x80\x99m sick to death of you all and I want to be alone,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Raskolnikov answered calmly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlone? Own up at once!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me go!\xe2\x80\x9d said Raskolnikov and tried to pass him. This was too much\r\nfor Razumihin; he gripped him firmly by the shoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet you go? You dare tell me to let you go? Do you know what I\xe2\x80\x99ll do\r\nwith you directly? I\xe2\x80\x99ll pick you up, tie you up in a bundle, carry you\r\nhome under my arm and lock you up!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cListen, Razumihin,\xe2\x80\x9d Raskolnikov began quietly, apparently calm--\xe2\x80\x9ccan\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou see that I don\xe2\x80\x99t want your benevolence? A strange desire you have to\r\nshower benefits on a man who... curses them, who feels them a burden in\r\nfact! Why did you seek me out at the beginning of my illness? Maybe I\r\nwas very glad to die. Didn\xe2\x80\x99t I tell you plainly enough to-day that\r\nyou were torturing me, that I was... sick of you! You seem to want to\r\ntorture people! I assure you that all that is seriously hindering my\r\nrecovery, because it\xe2\x80\x99s continually irritating me. You saw Zossimov\r\nwent away just now to avoid irritating me. You leave me alone too, for\r\ngoodness\xe2\x80\x99 sake! What right have you, indeed, to keep me by force? Don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou see that I am in possession of all my faculties now? How, how can\r\nI persuade you not to persecute me with your kindness? I may be\r\nungrateful, I may be mean, only let me be, for God\xe2\x80\x99s sake, let me be!\r\nLet me be, let me be!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe began calmly, gloating beforehand over the venomous phrases he was\r\nabout to utter, but finished, panting for breath, in a frenzy, as he had\r\nbeen with Luzhin.\r\n\r\nRazumihin stood a moment, thought and let his hand drop.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, go to hell then,\xe2\x80\x9d he said gently and thoughtfully. \xe2\x80\x9cStay,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nroared, as Raskolnikov was about to move. \xe2\x80\x9cListen to me. Let me tell\r\nyou, that you are all a set of babbling, posing idiots! If you\xe2\x80\x99ve any\r\nlittle trouble you brood over it like a hen over an egg. And you are\r\nplagiarists even in that! There isn\xe2\x80\x99t a sign of independent life in\r\nyou! You are made of spermaceti ointment and you\xe2\x80\x99ve lymph in your veins\r\ninstead of blood. I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe in anyone of you! In any circumstances\r\nthe first thing for all of you is to be unlike a human being! Stop!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\ncried with redoubled fury, noticing that Raskolnikov was again making\r\na movement--\xe2\x80\x9chear me out! You know I\xe2\x80\x99m having a house-warming this\r\nevening, I dare say they\xe2\x80\x99ve arrived by now, but I left my uncle there--I\r\njust ran in--to receive the guests. Since you have gone\r\nout, there\xe2\x80\x99s no help for it! I\xe2\x80\x99d give you a snug easy chair, my landlady\r\nhas one... a cup of tea, company.... Or you could lie on the sofa--any\r\nway you would be with us.... Zossimov will be there too. Will you come?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cR-rubbish!\xe2\x80\x9d Razumihin shouted, out of patience. \xe2\x80\x9cHow do you know?\r\nYou can\xe2\x80\x99t answer for yourself! You don\xe2\x80\x99t know anything about it....\r\nThousands of times I\xe2\x80\x99ve fought tooth and nail with people and run back\r\nto them afterwards.... One feels ashamed and goes back to a man! So\r\nremember, Potchinkov\xe2\x80\x99s house on the third storey....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Mr. Razumihin, I do believe you\xe2\x80\x99d let anybody beat you from sheer\r\nbenevolence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBeat?
