Potchinkov\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhouse, 47, Babushkin\xe2\x80\x99s flat....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall not come, Razumihin.\xe2\x80\x9d Raskolnikov turned and walked away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI bet you will,\xe2\x80\x9d Razumihin shouted after him. \xe2\x80\x9cI refuse to know you if\r\nyou don\xe2\x80\x99t! Stay, hey, is Zametov in there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you see him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTalked to him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat about? Confound you, don\xe2\x80\x99t tell me then. Potchinkov\xe2\x80\x99s house, 47,\r\nBabushkin\xe2\x80\x99s flat, remember!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov walked on and turned the corner into Sadovy Street.\r\nRazumihin looked after him thoughtfully. Then with a wave of his hand he\r\nwent into the house but stopped short of the stairs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cConfound it,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on almost aloud. \xe2\x80\x9cHe talked sensibly but yet...\r\nI am a fool! As if madmen didn\xe2\x80\x99t talk sensibly! And this was just what\r\nZossimov seemed afraid of.\xe2\x80\x9d He struck his finger on his forehead. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\r\nif... how could I let him go off alone? He may drown himself.... Ach,\r\nwhat a blunder! I can\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d And he ran back to overtake Raskolnikov, but\r\nthere was no trace of him. With a curse he returned with rapid steps to\r\nthe Palais de Cristal to question Zametov.\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov walked straight to X---- Bridge, stood in the middle, and\r\nleaning both elbows on the rail stared into the distance. On parting\r\nwith Razumihin, he felt so much weaker that he could scarcely reach this\r\nplace. He longed to sit or lie down somewhere in the street. Bending\r\nover the water, he gazed mechanically at the last pink flush of the\r\nsunset, at the row of houses growing dark in the gathering twilight, at\r\none distant attic window on the left bank, flashing as though on fire in\r\nthe last rays of the setting sun, at the darkening water of the canal,\r\nand the water seemed to catch his attention. At last red circles flashed\r\nbefore his eyes, the houses seemed moving, the passers-by, the canal\r\nbanks, the carriages, all danced before his eyes. Suddenly he started,\r\nsaved again perhaps from swooning by an uncanny and hideous sight. He\r\nbecame aware of someone standing on the right side of him; he looked\r\nand saw a tall woman with a kerchief on her head, with a long, yellow,\r\nwasted face and red sunken eyes. She was looking straight at him, but\r\nobviously she saw nothing and recognised no one. Suddenly she leaned her\r\nright hand on the parapet, lifted her right leg over the railing, then\r\nher left and threw herself into the canal. The filthy water parted and\r\nswallowed up its victim for a moment, but an instant later the drowning\r\nwoman floated to the surface, moving slowly with the current, her head\r\nand legs in the water, her skirt inflated like a balloon over her back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA woman drowning! A woman drowning!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted dozens of voices; people\r\nran up, both banks were thronged with spectators, on the bridge people\r\ncrowded about Raskolnikov, pressing up behind him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMercy on it! it\xe2\x80\x99s our Afrosinya!\xe2\x80\x9d a woman cried tearfully close by.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMercy! save her! kind people, pull her out!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA boat, a boat\xe2\x80\x9d was shouted in the crowd. But there was no need of a\r\nboat; a policeman ran down the steps to the canal, threw off his great\r\ncoat and his boots and rushed into the water. It was easy to reach her:\r\nshe floated within a couple of yards from the steps, he caught hold of\r\nher clothes with his right hand and with his left seized a pole which a\r\ncomrade held out to him; the drowning woman was pulled out at once. They\r\nlaid her on the granite pavement of the embankment. She soon recovered\r\nconsciousness, raised her head, sat up and began sneezing and coughing,\r\nstupidly wiping her wet dress with her hands. She said nothing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s drunk herself out of her senses,\xe2\x80\x9d the same woman\xe2\x80\x99s voice wailed\r\nat her side. \xe2\x80\x9cOut of her senses. The other day she tried to hang\r\nherself, we cut her down. I ran out to the shop just now, left my little\r\ngirl to look after her--and here she\xe2\x80\x99s in trouble again! A neighbour,\r\ngentleman, a neighbour, we live close by, the second house from the end,\r\nsee yonder....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe crowd broke up. The police still remained round the woman, someone\r\nmentioned the police station.... Raskolnikov looked on with a strange\r\nsensation of indifference and apathy. He felt disgusted. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nloathsome... water... it\xe2\x80\x99s not good enough,\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered to himself.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing will come of it,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, \xe2\x80\x9cno use to wait. What about the\r\npolice office...? And why isn\xe2\x80\x99t Zametov at the police office? The police\r\noffice is open till ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock....\xe2\x80\x9d He turned his back to the railing\r\nand looked about him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well then!\xe2\x80\x9d he said resolutely; he moved from the bridge and\r\nwalked in the direction of the police office. His heart felt hollow and\r\nempty. He did not want to think. Even his depression had passed, there\r\nwas not a trace now of the energy with which he had set out \xe2\x80\x9cto make an\r\nend of it all.\xe2\x80\x9d Complete apathy had succeeded to it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it\xe2\x80\x99s a way out of it,\xe2\x80\x9d he thought, walking slowly and listlessly\r\nalong the canal bank. \xe2\x80\x9cAnyway I\xe2\x80\x99ll make an end, for I want to.... But\r\nis it a way out? What does it matter! There\xe2\x80\x99ll be the square yard of\r\nspace--ha! But what an end! Is it really the end? Shall I tell them or\r\nnot? Ah... damn! How tired I am! If I could find somewhere to sit or lie\r\ndown soon! What I am most ashamed of is its being so stupid. But I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ncare about that either! What idiotic ideas come into one\xe2\x80\x99s head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo reach the police office he had to go straight forward and take the\r\nsecond turning to the left. It was only a few paces away. But at the\r\nfirst turning he stopped and, after a minute\xe2\x80\x99s thought, turned into a\r\nside street and went two streets out of his way, possibly without any\r\nobject, or possibly to delay a minute and gain time. He walked, looking\r\nat the ground; suddenly someone seemed to whisper in his ear; he lifted\r\nhis head and saw that he was standing at the very gate of _the_ house.\r\nHe had not passed it, he had not been near it since _that_ evening.\r\nAn overwhelming, unaccountable prompting drew him on. He went into the\r\nhouse, passed through the gateway, then into the first entrance on the\r\nright, and began mounting the familiar staircase to the fourth storey.\r\nThe narrow, steep staircase was very dark. He stopped at each landing\r\nand looked round him with curiosity; on the first landing the framework\r\nof the window had been taken out. \xe2\x80\x9cThat wasn\xe2\x80\x99t so then,\xe2\x80\x9d he thought.\r\nHere was the flat on the second storey where Nikolay and Dmitri had been\r\nworking. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s shut up and the door newly painted. So it\xe2\x80\x99s to let.\xe2\x80\x9d Then\r\nthe third storey and the fourth. \xe2\x80\x9cHere!\xe2\x80\x9d He was perplexed to find the\r\ndoor of the flat wide open. There were men there, he could hear voices;\r\nhe had not expected that. After brief hesitation he mounted the last\r\nstairs and went into the flat. It, too, was being done up; there were\r\nworkmen in it. This seemed to amaze him; he somehow fancied that he\r\nwould find everything as he left it, even perhaps the corpses in the\r\nsame places on the floor. And now, bare walls, no furniture; it seemed\r\nstrange. He walked to the window and sat down on the window-sill. There\r\nwere two workmen, both young fellows, but one much younger than the\r\nother. They were papering the walls with a new white paper covered with\r\nlilac flowers, instead of the old, dirty, yellow one. Raskolnikov for\r\nsome reason felt horribly annoyed by this. He looked at the new paper\r\nwith dislike, as though he felt sorry to have it all so changed.\r\nThe workmen had obviously stayed beyond their time and now they were\r\nhurriedly rolling up their paper and getting ready to go home. They took\r\nno notice of Raskolnikov\xe2\x80\x99s coming in; they were talking. Raskolnikov\r\nfolded his arms and listened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe comes to me in the morning,\xe2\x80\x9d said the elder to the younger, \xe2\x80\x9cvery\r\nearly, all dressed up. \xe2\x80\x98Why are you preening and prinking?\xe2\x80\x99 says I. \xe2\x80\x98I\r\nam ready to do anything to please you, Tit Vassilitch!\xe2\x80\x99 That\xe2\x80\x99s a way of\r\ngoing on! And she dressed up like a regular fashion book!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what is a fashion book?\xe2\x80\x9d the younger one asked. He obviously\r\nregarded the other as an authority.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA fashion book is a lot of pictures, coloured, and they come to the\r\ntailors here every Saturday, by post from abroad, to show folks how\r\nto dress, the male sex as well as the female. They\xe2\x80\x99re pictures. The\r\ngentlemen are generally wearing fur coats and for the ladies\xe2\x80\x99 fluffles,\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99re beyond anything you can fancy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s nothing you can\xe2\x80\x99t find in Petersburg,\xe2\x80\x9d the younger cried\r\nenthusiastically, \xe2\x80\x9cexcept father and mother, there\xe2\x80\x99s everything!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcept them, there\xe2\x80\x99s everything to be found, my boy,\xe2\x80\x9d the elder\r\ndeclared sententiously.\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov got up and walked into the other room where the strong box,\r\nthe bed, and the chest of drawers had been; the room seemed to him very\r\ntiny without furniture in it. The paper was the same; the paper in the\r\ncorner showed where the case of ikons had stood. He looked at it and\r\nwent to the window. The elder workman looked at him askance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you want?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked suddenly.\r\n\r\nInstead of answering Raskolnikov went into the passage and pulled the\r\nbell. The same bell, the same cracked note. He rang it a second and\r\na third time; he listened and remembered. The hideous and agonisingly\r\nfearful sensation he had felt then began to come back more and more\r\nvividly. He shuddered at every ring and it gave him more and more\r\nsatisfaction.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what do you want? Who are you?\xe2\x80\x9d the workman shouted, going out to\r\nhim. Raskolnikov went inside again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to take a flat,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI am looking round.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s not the time to look at rooms at night! and you ought to come up\r\nwith the porter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe floors have been washed, will they be painted?\xe2\x80\x9d Raskolnikov went\r\non. \xe2\x80\x9cIs there no blood?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat blood?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, the old woman and her sister were murdered here. There was a\r\nperfect pool there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut who are you?\xe2\x80\x9d the workman cried, uneasy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho am I?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou want to know? Come to the police station, I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe workmen looked at him in amazement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s time for us to go, we are late. Come along, Alyoshka. We must lock\r\nup,\xe2\x80\x9d said the elder workman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, come along,\xe2\x80\x9d said Raskolnikov indifferently, and going\r\nout first, he went slowly downstairs. \xe2\x80\x9cHey, porter,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried in the\r\ngateway.\r\n\r\nAt the entrance several people were standing, staring at the passers-by;\r\nthe two porters, a peasant woman, a man in a long coat and a few others.\r\nRaskolnikov went straight up to them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you want?\xe2\x80\x9d asked one of the porters.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you been to the police office?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve just been there. What do you want?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it open?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs the assistant there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was there for a time. What do you want?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov made no reply, but stood beside them lost in thought.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s been to look at the flat,\xe2\x80\x9d said the elder workman, coming forward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich flat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere we are at work. \xe2\x80\x98Why have you washed away the blood?\xe2\x80\x99 says he.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98There has been a murder here,\xe2\x80\x99 says he, \xe2\x80\x98and I\xe2\x80\x99ve come to take it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nAnd he began ringing at the bell, all but broke it. \xe2\x80\x98Come to the police\r\nstation,\xe2\x80\x99 says he. \xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you everything there.\xe2\x80\x99 He wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t leave\r\nus.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe porter looked at Raskolnikov, frowning and perplexed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho are you?\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted as impressively as he could.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am Rodion Romanovitch Raskolnikov, formerly a student, I live in\r\nShil\xe2\x80\x99s house, not far from here, flat Number 14, ask the porter, he\r\nknows me.\xe2\x80\x9d Raskolnikov said all this in a lazy, dreamy voice, not\r\nturning round, but looking intently into the darkening street.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy have you been to the flat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo look at it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is there to look at?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake him straight to the police station,\xe2\x80\x9d the man in the long coat\r\njerked in abruptly.\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov looked intently at him over his shoulder and said in the\r\nsame slow, lazy tones:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome along.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, take him,\xe2\x80\x9d the man went on more confidently. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy was he going\r\ninto _that_, what\xe2\x80\x99s in his mind, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s not drunk, but God knows what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with him,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered the\r\nworkman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what do you want?\xe2\x80\x9d the porter shouted again, beginning to get angry\r\nin earnest--\xe2\x80\x9cWhy are you hanging about?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou funk the police station then?\xe2\x80\x9d said Raskolnikov jeeringly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow funk it? Why are you hanging about?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a rogue!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted the peasant woman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy waste time talking to him?\xe2\x80\x9d cried the other porter, a huge peasant\r\nin a full open coat and with keys on his belt. \xe2\x80\x9cGet along! He is a rogue\r\nand no mistake. Get along!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd seizing Raskolnikov by the shoulder he flung him into the street. He\r\nlurched forward, but recovered his footing, looked at the spectators in\r\nsilence and walked away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStrange man!\xe2\x80\x9d observed the workman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are strange folks about nowadays,\xe2\x80\x9d said the woman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou should have taken him to the police station all the same,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nman in the long coat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetter have nothing to do with him,\xe2\x80\x9d decided the big porter. \xe2\x80\x9cA regular\r\nrogue! Just what he wants, you may be sure, but once take him up, you\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t get rid of him.... We know the sort!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I go there or not?\xe2\x80\x9d thought Raskolnikov, standing in the middle\r\nof the thoroughfare at the cross-roads, and he looked about him, as\r\nthough expecting from someone a decisive word. But no sound came, all\r\nwas dead and silent like the stones on which he walked, dead to him, to\r\nhim alone.... All at once at the end of the street, two hundred yards\r\naway, in the gathering dusk he saw a crowd and heard talk and shouts.\r\nIn the middle of the crowd stood a carriage.... A light gleamed in the\r\nmiddle of the street. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d Raskolnikov turned to the right\r\nand went up to the crowd. He seemed to clutch at everything and smiled\r\ncoldly when he recognised it, for he had fully made up his mind to go to\r\nthe police station and knew that it would all soon be over.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII\r\n\r\nAn elegant carriage stood in the middle of the road with a pair of\r\nspirited grey horses; there was no one in it, and the coachman had got\r\noff his box and stood by; the horses were being held by the bridle....\r\nA mass of people had gathered round, the police standing in front. One\r\nof them held a lighted lantern which he was turning on something lying\r\nclose to the wheels. Everyone was talking, shouting, exclaiming; the\r\ncoachman seemed at a loss and kept repeating:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a misfortune! Good Lord, what a misfortune!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov pushed his way in as far as he could, and succeeded at last\r\nin seeing the object of the commotion and interest. On the ground a\r\nman who had been run over lay apparently unconscious, and covered with\r\nblood; he was very badly dressed, but not like a workman. Blood was\r\nflowing from his head and face; his face was crushed, mutilated and\r\ndisfigured. He was evidently badly injured.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMerciful heaven!\xe2\x80\x9d wailed the coachman, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat more could I do? If I\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nbeen driving fast or had not shouted to him, but I was going quietly,\r\nnot in a hurry. Everyone could see I was going along just like everybody\r\nelse. A drunken man can\xe2\x80\x99t walk straight, we all know.... I saw him\r\ncrossing the street, staggering and almost falling. I shouted again\r\nand a second and a third time, then I held the horses in, but he fell\r\nstraight under their feet! Either he did it on purpose or he was very\r\ntipsy.... The horses are young and ready to take fright... they started,\r\nhe screamed... that made them worse. That\xe2\x80\x99s how it happened!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s just how it was,\xe2\x80\x9d a voice in the crowd confirmed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe shouted, that\xe2\x80\x99s true, he shouted three times,\xe2\x80\x9d another voice\r\ndeclared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree times it was, we all heard it,\xe2\x80\x9d shouted a third.\r\n\r\nBut the coachman was not very much distressed and frightened. It was\r\nevident that the carriage belonged to a rich and important person who\r\nwas awaiting it somewhere; the police, of course, were in no little\r\nanxiety to avoid upsetting his arrangements. All they had to do was to\r\ntake the injured man to the police station and the hospital. No one knew\r\nhis name.\r\n\r\nMeanwhile Raskolnikov had squeezed in and stooped closer over him. The\r\nlantern suddenly lighted up the unfortunate man\xe2\x80\x99s face. He recognised\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know him! I know him!\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted, pushing to the front. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\ngovernment clerk retired from the service, Marmeladov. He lives close\r\nby in Kozel\xe2\x80\x99s house.... Make haste for a doctor! I will pay, see?\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\npulled money out of his pocket and showed it to the policeman. He was in\r\nviolent agitation.\r\n\r\nThe police were glad that they had found out who the man was.\r\nRaskolnikov gave his own name and address, and, as earnestly as if it\r\nhad been his father, he besought the police to carry the unconscious\r\nMarmeladov to his lodging at once.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust here, three houses away,\xe2\x80\x9d he said eagerly, \xe2\x80\x9cthe house belongs to\r\nKozel, a rich German. He was going home, no doubt drunk. I know him,\r\nhe is a drunkard. He has a family there, a wife, children, he has one\r\ndaughter.... It will take time to take him to the hospital, and there is\r\nsure to be a doctor in the house. I\xe2\x80\x99ll pay, I\xe2\x80\x99ll pay! At least he will\r\nbe looked after at home... they will help him at once. But he\xe2\x80\x99ll die\r\nbefore you get him to the hospital.\xe2\x80\x9d He managed to slip something\r\nunseen into the policeman\xe2\x80\x99s hand. But the thing was straightforward\r\nand legitimate, and in any case help was closer here. They raised the\r\ninjured man; people volunteered to help.\r\n\r\nKozel\xe2\x80\x99s house was thirty yards away. Raskolnikov walked behind,\r\ncarefully holding Marmeladov\xe2\x80\x99s head and showing the way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis way, this way! We must take him upstairs head foremost. Turn\r\nround! I\xe2\x80\x99ll pay, I\xe2\x80\x99ll make it worth your while,\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered.\r\n\r\nKaterina Ivanovna had just begun, as she always did at every free\r\nmoment, walking to and fro in her little room from window to stove and\r\nback again, with her arms folded across her chest, talking to herself\r\nand coughing. Of late she had begun to talk more than ever to her eldest\r\ngirl, Polenka, a child of ten, who, though there was much she did not\r\nunderstand, understood very well that her mother needed her, and so\r\nalways watched her with her big clever eyes and strove her utmost\r\nto appear to understand. This time Polenka was undressing her little\r\nbrother, who had been unwell all day and was going to bed. The boy was\r\nwaiting for her to take off his shirt, which had to be washed at night.\r\nHe was sitting straight and motionless on a chair, with a silent,\r\nserious face, with his legs stretched out straight before him--heels\r\ntogether and toes turned out.\r\n\r\nHe was listening to what his mother was saying to his sister, sitting\r\nperfectly still with pouting lips and wide-open eyes, just as all good\r\nlittle boys have to sit when they are undressed to go to bed. A little\r\ngirl, still younger, dressed literally in rags, stood at the screen,\r\nwaiting for her turn. The door on to the stairs was open to relieve\r\nthem a little from the clouds of tobacco smoke which floated in from the\r\nother rooms and brought on long terrible fits of coughing in the poor,\r\nconsumptive woman. Katerina Ivanovna seemed to have grown even thinner\r\nduring that week and the hectic flush on her face was brighter than\r\never.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t believe, you can\xe2\x80\x99t imagine, Polenka,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, walking\r\nabout the room, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat a happy luxurious life we had in my papa\xe2\x80\x99s house\r\nand how this drunkard has brought me, and will bring you all, to ruin!\r\nPapa was a civil colonel and only a step from being a governor; so that\r\neveryone who came to see him said, \xe2\x80\x98We look upon you, Ivan Mihailovitch,\r\nas our governor!\xe2\x80\x99 When I... when...\xe2\x80\x9d she coughed violently, \xe2\x80\x9coh, cursed\r\nlife,\xe2\x80\x9d she cried, clearing her throat and pressing her hands to her\r\nbreast, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen I... when at the last ball... at the marshal\xe2\x80\x99s...\r\nPrincess Bezzemelny saw me--who gave me the blessing when your father\r\nand I were married, Polenka--she asked at once \xe2\x80\x98Isn\xe2\x80\x99t that the pretty\r\ngirl who danced the shawl dance at the breaking-up?\xe2\x80\x99 (You must mend\r\nthat tear, you must take your needle and darn it as I showed you, or\r\nto-morrow--cough, cough, cough--he will make the hole bigger,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\narticulated with effort.) \xe2\x80\x9cPrince Schegolskoy, a kammerjunker, had just\r\ncome from Petersburg then... he danced the mazurka with me and wanted to\r\nmake me an offer next day; but I thanked him in flattering expressions\r\nand told him that my heart had long been another\xe2\x80\x99s. That other was your\r\nfather, Polya; papa was fearfully angry.... Is the water ready? Give me\r\nthe shirt, and the stockings! Lida,\xe2\x80\x9d said she to the youngest one, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\r\nmust manage without your chemise to-night... and lay your stockings out\r\nwith it... I\xe2\x80\x99ll wash them together.... How is it that drunken vagabond\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t come in? He has worn his shirt till it looks like a dish-clout,\r\nhe has torn it to rags! I\xe2\x80\x99d do it all together, so as not to have to\r\nwork two nights running! Oh, dear! (Cough, cough, cough, cough!) Again!\r\nWhat\xe2\x80\x99s this?\xe2\x80\x9d she cried, noticing a crowd in the passage and the men,\r\nwho were pushing into her room, carrying a burden. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it? What are\r\nthey bringing? Mercy on us!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere are we to put him?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the policeman, looking round when\r\nMarmeladov, unconscious and covered with blood, had been carried in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn the sofa! Put him straight on the sofa, with his head this way,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Raskolnikov showed him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRun over in the road! Drunk!\xe2\x80\x9d someone shouted in the passage.\r\n\r\nKaterina Ivanovna stood, turning white and gasping for breath. The\r\nchildren were terrified. Little Lida screamed, rushed to Polenka and\r\nclutched at her, trembling all over.\r\n\r\nHaving laid Marmeladov down, Raskolnikov flew to Katerina Ivanovna.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor God\xe2\x80\x99s sake be calm, don\xe2\x80\x99t be frightened!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, speaking\r\nquickly, \xe2\x80\x9che was crossing the road and was run over by a carriage, don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbe frightened, he will come to, I told them bring him here... I\xe2\x80\x99ve been\r\nhere already, you remember? He will come to; I\xe2\x80\x99ll pay!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s done it this time!\xe2\x80\x9d Katerina Ivanovna cried despairingly and she\r\nrushed to her husband.\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov noticed at once that she was not one of those women who\r\nswoon easily. She instantly placed under the luckless man\xe2\x80\x99s head a\r\npillow, which no one had thought of and began undressing and examining\r\nhim. She kept her head, forgetting herself, biting her trembling lips\r\nand stifling the screams which were ready to break from her.\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov meanwhile induced someone to run for a doctor. There was a\r\ndoctor, it appeared, next door but one.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve sent for a doctor,\xe2\x80\x9d he kept assuring Katerina Ivanovna, \xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nuneasy, I\xe2\x80\x99ll pay. Haven\xe2\x80\x99t you water?... and give me a napkin or a towel,\r\nanything, as quick as you can.... He is injured, but not killed, believe\r\nme.... We shall see what the doctor says!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKaterina Ivanovna ran to the window; there, on a broken chair in the\r\ncorner, a large earthenware basin full of water had been stood, in\r\nreadiness for washing her children\xe2\x80\x99s and husband\xe2\x80\x99s linen that night.\r\nThis washing was done by Katerina Ivanovna at night at least twice a\r\nweek, if not oftener. For the family had come to such a pass that they\r\nwere practically without change of linen, and Katerina Ivanovna could\r\nnot endure uncleanliness and, rather than see dirt in the house, she\r\npreferred to wear herself out at night, working beyond her strength when\r\nthe rest were asleep, so as to get the wet linen hung on a line and dry\r\nby the morning. She took up the basin of water at Raskolnikov\xe2\x80\x99s request,\r\nbut almost fell down with her burden. But the latter had already\r\nsucceeded in finding a towel, wetted it and began washing the blood off\r\nMarmeladov\xe2\x80\x99s face.\r\n\r\nKaterina Ivanovna stood by, breathing painfully and pressing her hands\r\nto her breast. She was in need of attention herself. Raskolnikov began\r\nto realise that he might have made a mistake in having the injured man\r\nbrought here. The policeman, too, stood in hesitation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPolenka,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Katerina Ivanovna, \xe2\x80\x9crun to Sonia, make haste. If you\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t find her at home, leave word that her father has been run over\r\nand that she is to come here at once... when she comes in. Run, Polenka!\r\nthere, put on the shawl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRun your fastest!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the little boy on the chair suddenly, after\r\nwhich he relapsed into the same dumb rigidity, with round eyes, his\r\nheels thrust forward and his toes spread out.\r\n\r\nMeanwhile the room had become so full of people that you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t have\r\ndropped a pin. The policemen left, all except one, who remained for a\r\ntime, trying to drive out the people who came in from the stairs. Almost\r\nall Madame Lippevechsel\xe2\x80\x99s lodgers had streamed in from the inner rooms\r\nof the flat; at first they were squeezed together in the doorway, but\r\nafterwards they overflowed into the room. Katerina Ivanovna flew into a\r\nfury.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou might let him die in peace, at least,\xe2\x80\x9d she shouted at the crowd,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cis it a spectacle for you to gape at? With cigarettes! (Cough, cough,\r\ncough!) You might as well keep your hats on.... And there is one in his\r\nhat!... Get away! You should respect the dead, at least!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer cough choked her--but her reproaches were not without result. They\r\nevidently stood in some awe of Katerina Ivanovna. The lodgers, one after\r\nanother, squeezed back into the doorway with that strange inner feeling\r\nof satisfaction which may be observed in the presence of a sudden\r\naccident, even in those nearest and dearest to the victim, from which\r\nno living man is exempt, even in spite of the sincerest sympathy and\r\ncompassion.\r\n\r\nVoices outside were heard, however, speaking of the hospital and saying\r\nthat they\xe2\x80\x99d no business to make a disturbance here.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo business to die!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Katerina Ivanovna, and she was rushing to\r\nthe door to vent her wrath upon them, but in the doorway came face to\r\nface with Madame Lippevechsel who had only just heard of the accident\r\nand ran in to restore order. She was a particularly quarrelsome and\r\nirresponsible German.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, my God!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried, clasping her hands, \xe2\x80\x9cyour husband drunken\r\nhorses have trampled! To the hospital with him! I am the landlady!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAmalia Ludwigovna, I beg you to recollect what you are saying,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Katerina Ivanovna began haughtily (she always took a haughty tone with\r\nthe landlady that she might \xe2\x80\x9cremember her place\xe2\x80\x9d and even now could not\r\ndeny herself this satisfaction). \xe2\x80\x9cAmalia Ludwigovna...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have you once before told that you to call me Amalia Ludwigovna may\r\nnot dare; I am Amalia Ivanovna.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are not Amalia Ivanovna, but Amalia Ludwigovna, and as I am not\r\none of your despicable flatterers like Mr. Lebeziatnikov, who\xe2\x80\x99s laughing\r\nbehind the door at this moment (a laugh and a cry of \xe2\x80\x98they are at it\r\nagain\xe2\x80\x99 was in fact audible at the door) so I shall always call you\r\nAmalia Ludwigovna, though I fail to understand why you dislike that\r\nname. You can see for yourself what has happened to Semyon Zaharovitch;\r\nhe is dying. I beg you to close that door at once and to admit no one.\r\nLet him at least die in peace! Or I warn you the Governor-General,\r\nhimself, shall be informed of your conduct to-morrow. The prince knew\r\nme as a girl; he remembers Semyon Zaharovitch well and has often been\r\na benefactor to him. Everyone knows that Semyon Zaharovitch had many\r\nfriends and protectors, whom he abandoned himself from an honourable\r\npride, knowing his unhappy weakness, but now (she pointed to\r\nRaskolnikov) a generous young man has come to our assistance, who has\r\nwealth and connections and whom Semyon Zaharovitch has known from a\r\nchild. You may rest assured, Amalia Ludwigovna...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll this was uttered with extreme rapidity, getting quicker and quicker,\r\nbut a cough suddenly cut short Katerina Ivanovna\xe2\x80\x99s eloquence. At that\r\ninstant the dying man recovered consciousness and uttered a groan; she\r\nran to him. The injured man opened his eyes and without recognition or\r\nunderstanding gazed at Raskolnikov who was bending over him. He drew\r\ndeep, slow, painful breaths; blood oozed at the corners of his mouth\r\nand drops of perspiration came out on his forehead. Not recognising\r\nRaskolnikov, he began looking round uneasily. Katerina Ivanovna looked\r\nat him with a sad but stern face, and tears trickled from her eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy God! His whole chest is crushed! How he is bleeding,\xe2\x80\x9d she said\r\nin despair. \xe2\x80\x9cWe must take off his clothes. Turn a little, Semyon\r\nZaharovitch, if you can,\xe2\x80\x9d she cried to him.\r\n\r\nMarmeladov recognised her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA priest,\xe2\x80\x9d he articulated huskily.\r\n\r\nKaterina Ivanovna walked to the window, laid her head against the window\r\nframe and exclaimed in despair:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, cursed life!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA priest,\xe2\x80\x9d the dying man said again after a moment\xe2\x80\x99s silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99ve gone for him,\xe2\x80\x9d Katerina Ivanovna shouted to him, he obeyed her\r\nshout and was silent. With sad and timid eyes he looked for her; she\r\nreturned and stood by his pillow. He seemed a little easier but not for\r\nlong.\r\n\r\nSoon his eyes rested on little Lida, his favourite, who was shaking in\r\nthe corner, as though she were in a fit, and staring at him with her\r\nwondering childish eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA-ah,\xe2\x80\x9d he signed towards her uneasily. He wanted to say something.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat now?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Katerina Ivanovna.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBarefoot, barefoot!\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered, indicating with frenzied eyes the\r\nchild\xe2\x80\x99s bare feet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe silent,\xe2\x80\x9d Katerina Ivanovna cried irritably, \xe2\x80\x9cyou know why she is\r\nbarefooted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank God, the doctor,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Raskolnikov, relieved.\r\n\r\nThe doctor came in, a precise little old man, a German, looking about\r\nhim mistrustfully; he went up to the sick man, took his pulse, carefully\r\nfelt his head and with the help of Katerina Ivanovna he unbuttoned the\r\nblood-stained shirt, and bared the injured man\xe2\x80\x99s chest. It was gashed,\r\ncrushed and fractured, several ribs on the right side were broken.\r\nOn the left side, just over the heart, was a large, sinister-looking\r\nyellowish-black bruise--a cruel kick from the horse\xe2\x80\x99s hoof. The doctor\r\nfrowned. The policeman told him that he was caught in the wheel and\r\nturned round with it for thirty yards on the road.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s wonderful that he has recovered consciousness,\xe2\x80\x9d the doctor\r\nwhispered softly to Raskolnikov.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you think of him?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will die immediately.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs there really no hope?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot the faintest! He is at the last gasp.... His head is badly injured,\r\ntoo... Hm... I could bleed him if you like, but... it would be useless.\r\nHe is bound to die within the next five or ten minutes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetter bleed him then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you like.... But I warn you it will be perfectly useless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt that moment other steps were heard; the crowd in the passage parted,\r\nand the priest, a little, grey old man, appeared in the doorway bearing\r\nthe sacrament. A policeman had gone for him at the time of the accident.\r\nThe doctor changed places with him, exchanging glances with him.\r\nRaskolnikov begged the doctor to remain a little while. He shrugged his\r\nshoulders and remained.\r\n\r\nAll stepped back. The confession was soon over. The dying man probably\r\nunderstood little; he could only utter indistinct broken sounds.\r\nKaterina Ivanovna took little Lida, lifted the boy from the chair, knelt\r\ndown in the corner by the stove and made the children kneel in front of\r\nher. The little girl was still trembling; but the boy, kneeling on his\r\nlittle bare knees, lifted his hand rhythmically, crossing himself with\r\nprecision and bowed down, touching the floor with his forehead, which\r\nseemed to afford him especial satisfaction. Katerina Ivanovna bit her\r\nlips and held back her tears; she prayed, too, now and then pulling\r\nstraight the boy\xe2\x80\x99s shirt, and managed to cover the girl\xe2\x80\x99s bare shoulders\r\nwith a kerchief, which she took from the chest without rising from her\r\nknees or ceasing to pray. Meanwhile the door from the inner rooms was\r\nopened inquisitively again. In the passage the crowd of spectators from\r\nall the flats on the staircase grew denser and denser, but they did not\r\nventure beyond the threshold. A single candle-end lighted up the scene.\r\n\r\nAt that moment Polenka forced her way through the crowd at the door. She\r\ncame in panting from running so fast, took off her kerchief, looked for\r\nher mother, went up to her and said, \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s coming, I met her in the\r\nstreet.\xe2\x80\x9d Her mother made her kneel beside her.\r\n\r\nTimidly and noiselessly a young girl made her way through the crowd,\r\nand strange was her appearance in that room, in the midst of want, rags,\r\ndeath and despair. She, too, was in rags, her attire was all of\r\nthe cheapest, but decked out in gutter finery of a special stamp,\r\nunmistakably betraying its shameful purpose. Sonia stopped short in the\r\ndoorway and looked about her bewildered, unconscious of everything.\r\nShe forgot her fourth-hand, gaudy silk dress, so unseemly here with\r\nits ridiculous long train, and her immense crinoline that filled up the\r\nwhole doorway, and her light-coloured shoes, and the parasol she brought\r\nwith her, though it was no use at night, and the absurd round straw hat\r\nwith its flaring flame-coloured feather. Under this rakishly-tilted hat\r\nwas a pale, frightened little face with lips parted and eyes staring in\r\nterror. Sonia was a small thin girl of eighteen with fair hair, rather\r\npretty, with wonderful blue eyes. She looked intently at the bed and the\r\npriest; she too was out of breath with running. At last whispers, some\r\nwords in the crowd probably, reached her. She looked down and took a\r\nstep forward into the room, still keeping close to the door.\r\n\r\nThe service was over. Katerina Ivanovna went up to her husband again.\r\nThe priest stepped back and turned to say a few words of admonition and\r\nconsolation to Katerina Ivanovna on leaving.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat am I to do with these?\xe2\x80\x9d she interrupted sharply and irritably,\r\npointing to the little ones.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod is merciful; look to the Most High for succour,\xe2\x80\x9d the priest began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAch! He is merciful, but not to us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a sin, a sin, madam,\xe2\x80\x9d observed the priest, shaking his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd isn\xe2\x80\x99t that a sin?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Katerina Ivanovna, pointing to the dying\r\nman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps those who have involuntarily caused the accident will agree to\r\ncompensate you, at least for the loss of his earnings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t understand!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Katerina Ivanovna angrily waving her hand.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd why should they compensate me? Why, he was drunk and threw himself\r\nunder the horses! What earnings? He brought us in nothing but misery.\r\nHe drank everything away, the drunkard! He robbed us to get drink, he\r\nwasted their lives and mine for drink! And thank God he\xe2\x80\x99s dying! One\r\nless to keep!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must forgive in the hour of death, that\xe2\x80\x99s a sin, madam, such\r\nfeelings are a great sin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKaterina Ivanovna was busy with the dying man; she was giving him water,\r\nwiping the blood and sweat from his head, setting his pillow straight,\r\nand had only turned now and then for a moment to address the priest. Now\r\nshe flew at him almost in a frenzy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, father! That\xe2\x80\x99s words and only words! Forgive! If he\xe2\x80\x99d not been run\r\nover, he\xe2\x80\x99d have come home to-day drunk and his only shirt dirty and\r\nin rags and he\xe2\x80\x99d have fallen asleep like a log, and I should have been\r\nsousing and rinsing till daybreak, washing his rags and the children\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nand then drying them by the window and as soon as it was daylight I\r\nshould have been darning them. That\xe2\x80\x99s how I spend my nights!... What\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthe use of talking of forgiveness! I have forgiven as it is!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA terrible hollow cough interrupted her words. She put her handkerchief\r\nto her lips and showed it to the priest, pressing her other hand to her\r\naching chest. The handkerchief was covered with blood. The priest bowed\r\nhis head and said nothing.\r\n\r\nMarmeladov was in the last agony; he did not take his eyes off the face\r\nof Katerina Ivanovna, who was bending over him again. He kept trying\r\nto say something to her; he began moving his tongue with difficulty and\r\narticulating indistinctly, but Katerina Ivanovna, understanding that he\r\nwanted to ask her forgiveness, called peremptorily to him:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe silent! No need! I know what you want to say!\xe2\x80\x9d And the sick man\r\nwas silent, but at the same instant his wandering eyes strayed to the\r\ndoorway and he saw Sonia.\r\n\r\nTill then he had not noticed her: she was standing in the shadow in a\r\ncorner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99s that? Who\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d he said suddenly in a thick gasping voice,\r\nin agitation, turning his eyes in horror towards the door where his\r\ndaughter was standing, and trying to sit up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLie down! Lie do-own!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Katerina Ivanovna.\r\n\r\nWith unnatural strength he had succeeded in propping himself on his\r\nelbow. He looked wildly and fixedly for some time on his daughter, as\r\nthough not recognising her. He had never seen her before in such attire.\r\nSuddenly he recognised her, crushed and ashamed in her humiliation and\r\ngaudy finery, meekly awaiting her turn to say good-bye to her dying\r\nfather. His face showed intense suffering.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSonia! Daughter! Forgive!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried, and he tried to hold out his hand\r\nto her, but losing his balance, he fell off the sofa, face downwards on\r\nthe floor. They rushed to pick him up, they put him on the sofa; but he\r\nwas dying. Sonia with a faint cry ran up, embraced him and remained so\r\nwithout moving. He died in her arms.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s got what he wanted,\xe2\x80\x9d Katerina Ivanovna cried, seeing her husband\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndead body. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, what\xe2\x80\x99s to be done now? How am I to bury him! What can\r\nI give them to-morrow to eat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov went up to Katerina Ivanovna.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKaterina Ivanovna,\xe2\x80\x9d he began, \xe2\x80\x9clast week your husband told me all his\r\nlife and circumstances.... Believe me, he spoke of you with passionate\r\nreverence. From that evening, when I learnt how devoted he was to you\r\nall and how he loved and respected you especially, Katerina Ivanovna,\r\nin spite of his unfortunate weakness, from that evening we became\r\nfriends.... Allow me now... to do something... to repay my debt to my\r\ndead friend. Here are twenty roubles, I think--and if that can be of any\r\nassistance to you, then... I... in short, I will come again, I will\r\nbe sure to come again... I shall, perhaps, come again to-morrow....\r\nGood-bye!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd he went quickly out of the room, squeezing his way through the crowd\r\nto the stairs. But in the crowd he suddenly jostled against Nikodim\r\nFomitch, who had heard of the accident and had come to give instructions\r\nin person. They had not met since the scene at the police station, but\r\nNikodim Fomitch knew him instantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, is that you?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s dead,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Raskolnikov. \xe2\x80\x9cThe doctor and the priest have been,\r\nall as it should have been. Don\xe2\x80\x99t worry the poor woman too much, she is\r\nin consumption as it is. Try and cheer her up, if possible... you are a\r\nkind-hearted man, I know...\xe2\x80\x9d he added with a smile, looking straight in\r\nhis face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you are spattered with blood,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Nikodim Fomitch, noticing\r\nin the lamplight some fresh stains on Raskolnikov\xe2\x80\x99s waistcoat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes... I\xe2\x80\x99m covered with blood,\xe2\x80\x9d Raskolnikov said with a peculiar air;\r\nthen he smiled, nodded and went downstairs.\r\n\r\nHe walked down slowly and deliberately, feverish but not conscious\r\nof it, entirely absorbed in a new overwhelming sensation of life and\r\nstrength that surged up suddenly within him. This sensation might be\r\ncompared to that of a man condemned to death who has suddenly been\r\npardoned. Halfway down the staircase he was overtaken by the priest on\r\nhis way home; Raskolnikov let him pass, exchanging a silent greeting\r\nwith him. He was just descending the last steps when he heard rapid\r\nfootsteps behind him. Someone overtook him; it was Polenka. She was\r\nrunning after him, calling \xe2\x80\x9cWait! wait!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe turned round. She was at the bottom of the staircase and stopped\r\nshort a step above him. A dim light came in from the yard. Raskolnikov\r\ncould distinguish the child\xe2\x80\x99s thin but pretty little face, looking at\r\nhim with a bright childish smile. She had run after him with a message\r\nwhich she was evidently glad to give.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell me, what is your name?... and where do you live?\xe2\x80\x9d she said\r\nhurriedly in a breathless voice.\r\n\r\nHe laid both hands on her shoulders and looked at her with a sort of\r\nrapture. It was such a joy to him to look at her, he could not have said\r\nwhy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho sent you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSister Sonia sent me,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the girl, smiling still more brightly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knew it was sister Sonia sent you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMamma sent me, too... when sister Sonia was sending me, mamma came up,\r\ntoo, and said \xe2\x80\x98Run fast, Polenka.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you love sister Sonia?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI love her more than anyone,\xe2\x80\x9d Polenka answered with a peculiar\r\nearnestness, and her smile became graver.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd will you love me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBy way of answer he saw the little girl\xe2\x80\x99s face approaching him, her full\r\nlips na\xc3\xafvely held out to kiss him. Suddenly her arms as thin as sticks\r\nheld him tightly, her head rested on his shoulder and the little girl\r\nwept softly, pressing her face against him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry for father,\xe2\x80\x9d she said a moment later, raising her\r\ntear-stained face and brushing away the tears with her hands. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnothing but misfortunes now,\xe2\x80\x9d she added suddenly with that peculiarly\r\nsedate air which children try hard to assume when they want to speak\r\nlike grown-up people.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid your father love you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe loved Lida most,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on very seriously without a smile,\r\nexactly like grown-up people, \xe2\x80\x9che loved her because she is little and\r\nbecause she is ill, too. And he always used to bring her presents. But\r\nhe taught us to read and me grammar and scripture, too,\xe2\x80\x9d she added with\r\ndignity. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd mother never used to say anything, but we knew that she\r\nliked it and father knew it, too. And mother wants to teach me French,\r\nfor it\xe2\x80\x99s time my education began.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd do you know your prayers?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, we do! We knew them long ago. I say my prayers to myself\r\nas I am a big girl now, but Kolya and Lida say them aloud with mother.\r\nFirst they repeat the \xe2\x80\x98Ave Maria\xe2\x80\x99 and then another prayer: \xe2\x80\x98Lord,\r\nforgive and bless sister Sonia,\xe2\x80\x99 and then another, \xe2\x80\x98Lord, forgive and\r\nbless our second father.\xe2\x80\x99 For our elder father is dead and this is\r\nanother one, but we do pray for the other as well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPolenka, my name is Rodion. Pray sometimes for me, too. \xe2\x80\x98And Thy\r\nservant Rodion,\xe2\x80\x99 nothing more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll pray for you all the rest of my life,\xe2\x80\x9d the little girl declared\r\nhotly, and suddenly smiling again she rushed at him and hugged him\r\nwarmly once more.\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov told her his name and address and promised to be sure to\r\ncome next day. The child went away quite enchanted with him. It was past\r\nten when he came out into the street. In five minutes he was standing on\r\nthe bridge at the spot where the woman had jumped in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEnough,\xe2\x80\x9d he pronounced resolutely and triumphantly. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve done with\r\nfancies, imaginary terrors and phantoms! Life is real! haven\xe2\x80\x99t I lived\r\njust now? My life has not yet died with that old woman! The Kingdom of\r\nHeaven to her--and now enough, madam, leave me in peace! Now for the\r\nreign of reason and light... and of will, and of strength... and now\r\nwe will see! We will try our strength!\xe2\x80\x9d he added defiantly, as though\r\nchallenging some power of darkness. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I was ready to consent to live\r\nin a square of space!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very weak at this moment, but... I believe my illness is all over.\r\nI knew it would be over when I went out. By the way, Potchinkov\xe2\x80\x99s house\r\nis only a few steps away. I certainly must go to Razumihin even if\r\nit were not close by... let him win his bet! Let us give him some\r\nsatisfaction, too--no matter! Strength, strength is what one wants, you\r\ncan get nothing without it, and strength must be won by strength--that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwhat they don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d he added proudly and self-confidently and\r\nhe walked with flagging footsteps from the bridge. Pride and\r\nself-confidence grew continually stronger in him; he was becoming\r\na different man every moment. What was it had happened to work this\r\nrevolution in him? He did not know himself; like a man catching at a\r\nstraw, he suddenly felt that he, too, \xe2\x80\x98could live, that there was still\r\nlife for him, that his life had not died with the old woman.\xe2\x80\x99 Perhaps he\r\nwas in too great a hurry with his conclusions, but he did not think of\r\nthat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I did ask her to remember \xe2\x80\x98Thy servant Rodion\xe2\x80\x99 in her prayers,\xe2\x80\x9d the\r\nidea struck him. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, that was... in case of emergency,\xe2\x80\x9d he added and\r\nlaughed himself at his boyish sally. He was in the best of spirits.\r\n\r\nHe easily found Razumihin; the new lodger was already known at\r\nPotchinkov\xe2\x80\x99s and the porter at once showed him the way. Half-way\r\nupstairs he could hear the noise and animated conversation of a big\r\ngathering of people. The door was wide open on the stairs; he could\r\nhear exclamations and discussion. Razumihin\xe2\x80\x99s room was fairly large; the\r\ncompany consisted of fifteen people. Raskolnikov stopped in the entry,\r\nwhere two of the landlady\xe2\x80\x99s servants were busy behind a screen with two\r\nsamovars, bottles, plates and dishes of pie and savouries, brought up\r\nfrom the landlady\xe2\x80\x99s kitchen. Raskolnikov sent in for Razumihin. He ran\r\nout delighted. At the first glance it was apparent that he had had a\r\ngreat deal to drink and, though no amount of liquor made Razumihin quite\r\ndrunk, this time he was perceptibly affected by it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cListen,\xe2\x80\x9d Raskolnikov hastened to say, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve only just come to tell you\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve won your bet and that no one really knows what may not happen to\r\nhim. I can\xe2\x80\x99t come in; I am so weak that I shall fall down directly. And\r\nso good evening and good-bye! Come and see me to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know what? I\xe2\x80\x99ll see you home. If you say you\xe2\x80\x99re weak yourself,\r\nyou must...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd your visitors? Who is the curly-headed one who has just peeped\r\nout?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe? Goodness only knows! Some friend of uncle\xe2\x80\x99s, I expect, or perhaps\r\nhe has come without being invited... I\xe2\x80\x99ll leave uncle with them, he\r\nis an invaluable person, pity I can\xe2\x80\x99t introduce you to him now. But\r\nconfound them all now! They won\xe2\x80\x99t notice me, and I need a little fresh\r\nair, for you\xe2\x80\x99ve come just in the nick of time--another two minutes and I\r\nshould have come to blows! They are talking such a lot of wild stuff...\r\nyou simply can\xe2\x80\x99t imagine what men will say! Though why shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\nimagine? Don\xe2\x80\x99t we talk nonsense ourselves? Wait a minute, I\xe2\x80\x99ll fetch Zossimov.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nZossimov pounced upon Raskolnikov almost greedily; he showed a special\r\ninterest in him; soon his face brightened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must go to bed at once,\xe2\x80\x9d he pronounced, examining the patient as\r\nfar as he could, \xe2\x80\x9cand take something for the night. Live and learn...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know what Zossimov whispered to me when we were coming out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Razumihin blurted out, as soon as they were in the street. \xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t tell\r\nyou everything, brother, because they are such fools. Only fancy! In the first place,\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve three times the brains he has; in the second, if you are not mad,\r\nyou needn\xe2\x80\x99t care a hang that he has got such a wild idea; and thirdly,\r\nthat piece of beef whose specialty is surgery has gone mad on mental\r\ndiseases, and what\xe2\x80\x99s brought him to this conclusion about you was your\r\nconversation to-day with Zametov.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cZametov told you all about it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and he did well. But that\xe2\x80\x99s... no matter... the point is that this\r\nidea... you understand? was just being hatched in their brains... you\r\nunderstand? That is, no one ventured to say it aloud, because the idea\r\nis too absurd and especially since the arrest of that painter, that\r\nbubble\xe2\x80\x99s burst and gone for ever. I gave\r\nZametov a bit of a thrashing at the time--that\xe2\x80\x99s between ourselves,\r\nbrother; please don\xe2\x80\x99t let out a hint that you know of it; I\xe2\x80\x99ve noticed\r\nhe is a ticklish subject; it was at Luise Ivanovna\xe2\x80\x99s. But don\xe2\x80\x99t you mind him...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor half a minute both were silent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cListen, Razumihin,\xe2\x80\x9d began Raskolnikov, \xe2\x80\x9cI want to tell you plainly:\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve just been at a death-bed, a clerk who died... Through the crack...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey were already at the foot of the last flight of stairs, at the level\r\nof the landlady\xe2\x80\x99s door, and they could, as a fact, see from below that\r\nthere was a light in Raskolnikov\xe2\x80\x99s garret.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQueer! Raskolnikov was the first to open the\r\ndoor; he flung it wide and stood still in the doorway, dumbfoundered.\r\n\r\nHis mother and sister were sitting on his sofa and had been waiting an\r\nhour and a half for him. Why had he never expected, never thought of\r\nthem, though the news that they had started, were on their way and would\r\narrive immediately, had been repeated to him only that day? They had\r\nspent that hour and a half plying Nastasya with questions. She was\r\nstanding before them and had told them everything by now. They were\r\nbeside themselves with alarm when they heard of his \xe2\x80\x9crunning away\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n to-day, ill and, as they understood from her story, delirious! \xe2\x80\x9cGood\r\nHeavens, what had become of him?\xe2\x80\x9d Both had been weeping, both had been\r\nin anguish for that hour and a half.\r\n\r\nA cry of joy, of ecstasy, greeted Raskolnikov\xe2\x80\x99s entrance. Both rushed to\r\nhim. But he stood like one dead; a sudden intolerable sensation struck\r\nhim like a thunderbolt. He did not lift his arms to embrace them, he\r\ncould not. His mother and sister clasped him in their arms, kissed him,\r\nlaughed and cried. He took a step, tottered and fell to the ground,\r\nfainting.\r\n\r\nAnxiety, cries of horror, moans... Razumihin who was standing in the\r\ndoorway flew into the room, seized the sick man in his strong arms and\r\nin a moment had him on the sofa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s nothing, nothing!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried to the mother and sister--\xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s only a\r\nfaint, a mere trifle! Only just now the doctor said he was much better,\r\nthat he is perfectly well! Water! See, he is coming to himself, he is\r\nall right again!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd seizing Dounia by the arm so that he almost dislocated it, he made\r\nher bend down to see that \xe2\x80\x9che is all right again.\xe2\x80\x9d The mother and sister\r\nlooked on him with emotion and gratitude, as their Providence. They\r\nhad heard already from Nastasya all that had been done for their Rodya\r\nduring his illness, by this \xe2\x80\x9cvery competent young man,\xe2\x80\x9d as Pulcheria\r\nAlexandrovna Raskolnikov called him that evening in conversation with\r\nDounia.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nPART III\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER I\r\n\r\nRaskolnikov got up, and sat down on the sofa. He waved his hand weakly\r\nto Razumihin to cut short the flow of warm and incoherent consolations\r\nhe was addressing to his mother and sister, took them both by the hand\r\nand for a minute or two gazed from one to the other without speaking.\r\nHis mother was alarmed by his expression. It revealed an emotion\r\nagonisingly poignant, and at the same time something immovable, almost\r\ninsane. Pulcheria Alexandrovna began to cry.\r\n\r\nAvdotya Romanovna was pale; her hand trembled in her brother\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo home... with him,\xe2\x80\x9d he said in a broken voice, pointing to Razumihin,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cgood-bye till to-morrow; to-morrow everything... Is it long since you\r\narrived?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis evening, Rodya,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Pulcheria Alexandrovna, \xe2\x80\x9cthe train was\r\nawfully late. I\r\nwill spend the night here, near you...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t torture me!\xe2\x80\x9d he said with a gesture of irritation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will stay with him,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Razumihin, \xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t leave him for a\r\nmoment. Bother all my visitors! Let them rage to their hearts\xe2\x80\x99 content!\r\nMy uncle is presiding there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow, how can I thank you!\xe2\x80\x9d Pulcheria Alexandrovna was beginning, once\r\nmore pressing Razumihin\xe2\x80\x99s hands, but Raskolnikov interrupted her again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t have it! That was so. And Luzhin got angry, too.... He made\r\nspeeches here, wanted to show off his learning and he went out\r\ncrest-fallen....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen it\xe2\x80\x99s true?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Pulcheria Alexandrovna.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye till to-morrow, brother,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dounia compassionately--\xe2\x80\x9clet us\r\ngo, mother... And he will slip off again if you irritate him, at this\r\ntime of night, and will do himself some mischief....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you saying?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Avdotya Romanovna can\xe2\x80\x99t possibly be left in those lodgings without\r\nyou. But you know I\xe2\x80\x99ve had a little to\r\ndrink, and that\xe2\x80\x99s what makes me... swear; don\xe2\x80\x99t mind it....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I\xe2\x80\x99ll go to the landlady here,\xe2\x80\x9d Pulcheria Alexandrovna insisted,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll beseech her to find some corner for Dounia and me for the night. I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t leave him like that, I cannot!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis conversation took place on the landing just before the landlady\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndoor. Nastasya lighted them from a step below. Razumihin was in\r\nextraordinary excitement. Half an hour earlier, while he was bringing\r\nRaskolnikov home, he had indeed talked too freely, but he was aware of\r\nit himself, and his head was clear in spite of the vast quantities he\r\nhad imbibed. Now he was in a state bordering on ecstasy, and all that he\r\nhad drunk seemed to fly to his head with redoubled effect. He stood with\r\nthe two ladies, seizing both by their hands, persuading them, and giving\r\nthem reasons with astonishing plainness of speech, and at almost every\r\nword he uttered, probably to emphasise his arguments, he squeezed their\r\nhands painfully as in a vise. He stared at Avdotya Romanovna without the\r\nleast regard for good manners. They sometimes pulled their hands out of\r\nhis huge bony paws, but far from noticing what was the matter, he drew\r\nthem all the closer to him. If they\xe2\x80\x99d told him to jump head foremost\r\nfrom the staircase, he would have done it without thought or hesitation\r\nin their service. Though Pulcheria Alexandrovna felt that the young man\r\nwas really too eccentric and pinched her hand too much, in her anxiety\r\nover her Rodya she looked on his presence as providential, and was\r\nunwilling to notice all his peculiarities. But though Avdotya Romanovna\r\nshared her anxiety, and was not of timorous disposition, she could not\r\nsee the glowing light in his eyes without wonder and almost alarm. It\r\nwas only the unbounded confidence inspired by Nastasya\xe2\x80\x99s account of her\r\nbrother\xe2\x80\x99s queer friend, which prevented her from trying to run away from\r\nhim, and to persuade her mother to do the same. She realised, too,\r\nthat even running away was perhaps impossible now. Ten minutes later,\r\nhowever, she was considerably reassured; it was characteristic of\r\nRazumihin that he showed his true nature at once, whatever mood he might\r\nbe in, so that people quickly saw the sort of man they had to deal with.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t go to the landlady, that\xe2\x80\x99s perfect nonsense!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried. \xe2\x80\x9cIf\r\nyou stay, though you are his mother, you\xe2\x80\x99ll drive him to a frenzy, and\r\nthen goodness knows what will happen! Yet apart from his eccentric condition, many\r\npeople would have thought it justified if they had seen Avdotya\r\nRomanovna, especially at that moment when she was walking to and\r\nfro with folded arms, pensive and melancholy. Avdotya Romanovna was\r\nremarkably good-looking; she was tall, strikingly well-proportioned,\r\nstrong and self-reliant--the latter quality was apparent in every\r\ngesture, though it did not in the least detract from the grace and\r\nsoftness of her movements. It was natural enough that a warm, open,\r\nsimple-hearted, honest giant like Razumihin, who had never seen anyone\r\nlike her and was not quite sober at the time, should lose his head\r\nimmediately. Although Pulcheria Alexandrovna was\r\nforty-three, her face still retained traces of her former beauty; she\r\nlooked much younger than her age, indeed, which is almost always the\r\ncase with women who retain serenity of spirit, sensitiveness and pure\r\nsincere warmth of heart to old age. We may add in parenthesis that to\r\npreserve all this is the only means of retaining beauty to old age. Her\r\nhair had begun to grow grey and thin, there had long been little crow\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfoot wrinkles round her eyes, her cheeks were hollow and sunken from\r\nanxiety and grief, and yet it was a handsome face. She was Dounia\r\nover again, twenty years older, but without the projecting underlip.\r\nPulcheria Alexandrovna was emotional, but not sentimental, timid and\r\nyielding, but only to a certain point. She could give way and accept a\r\ngreat deal even of what was contrary to her convictions, but there was a\r\ncertain barrier fixed by honesty, principle and the deepest convictions\r\nwhich nothing would induce her to cross.\r\n\r\nExactly twenty minutes after Razumihin\xe2\x80\x99s departure, there came two\r\nsubdued but hurried knocks at the door: he had come back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t come in, I haven\xe2\x80\x99t time,\xe2\x80\x9d he hastened to say when the door was\r\nopened. \xe2\x80\x9cHe sleeps like a top, soundly, quietly, and God grant he may\r\nsleep ten hours. Zossimov had agreed at once to desert the drinking party to\r\ngo to Raskolnikov\xe2\x80\x99s, but he came reluctantly and with the greatest\r\nsuspicion to see the ladies, mistrusting Razumihin in his exhilarated\r\ncondition. He stayed just ten\r\nminutes and succeeded in completely convincing and comforting Pulcheria\r\nAlexandrovna. He spoke with marked sympathy, but with the reserve and\r\nextreme seriousness of a young doctor at an important consultation.\r\nHe did not utter a word on any other subject and did not display the\r\nslightest desire to enter into more personal relations with the two\r\nladies. According to his\r\nobservations the patient\xe2\x80\x99s illness was due partly to his unfortunate\r\nmaterial surroundings during the last few months, but it had partly also\r\na moral origin, \xe2\x80\x9cwas, so to speak, the product of several material and\r\nmoral influences, anxieties, apprehensions, troubles, certain ideas...\r\nand so on.\xe2\x80\x9d Noticing stealthily that Avdotya Romanovna was following his\r\nwords with close attention, Zossimov allowed himself to enlarge on this\r\ntheme.
